Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Human Acts Are Created by Allah Alone
Dardeer states that all human actions are created by Allah, not by human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all human voluntary actions are directly created by Allah and how this aligns with the Ash'ari concept of acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
True creative power belongs only to Allah; human acts are His creation.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer applies the doctrine of divine unity to the question of human actions. He argues that since Allah alone possesses the power to bring things from non-existence into existence, no human power can have any real effect. This establishes the Ash'ari position that all human actions are created by Allah, while still affirming human accountability through the concept of acquisition (kasb). Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Dardeer uses the Quranic verse to seal his argument.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two clear sections: first, the general principle that tathīr (effective power) is only for Allah; second, the application to human actions, concluding with a Quranic proof.
These two parts are connected by the logic of divine unity.
3
Understand the implication for human responsibility
If our power has no effect, how are we accountable? Ash'aris answer with kasb (acquisition). The passage does not elaborate on kasb, but it sets the foundation: the act is Allah's creation, the human acquires it.
This prevents misunderstanding that humans are mere puppets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت أنه تعالى يجب له الوَحْدانيّةُ (فالتأثير) الاختراعُ والإيجاد للأشياء مِنَ العدَمِ (لَيسَ) أي: لا يصِحُ لأحَدٍ (إلا للواحِدِ القَهَّارِ) وحدَه (جل وعلا). فلا تأثيرَ لقُدرتِنا في شَيْءٍ مِنْ أفعالنا الاختيارية، كالحركات والسكنات والقيام والقعود ونحو ذلك، بل جميع ذلك مخلوق له سبحانه وتعالى بلا واسطة، كما أن قدرتنا مخلوقةٌ لَه تعالى، ﴿ وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ أَيْ : وخَلَقَ عَمَلَكم .
And when you know that He, exalted, must have unity, then the effective power (al-tathīr) — i.e., invention and bringing-into-existence of things from nothing — is not correct for anyone except the One, the Subduer (al-wāḥid al-qahhār) alone, may He be glorified and exalted. So there is no effect for our power in any of our voluntary actions, like movements and rests, standing and sitting, and the like. Rather, all of that is created by Him, glorified and exalted, without any intermediary, just as our power is created by Him, exalted. [And He said] 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Quran 37:96), i.e., He created your action.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage applies the principle of unity in acts to human actions. The author states that since Allah alone has true effective power (tathīr) — meaning invention and creation from nothing — no one else can have any real effect. Human power has no influence whatsoever on our voluntary actions such as movements, rest, sitting, standing. All such actions are directly created by Allah without mediation, just as our power itself is created. He cites the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' to support this, interpreting 'what you do' as 'your action' (i.e., the act itself is created). This is the Ash'ari position on kasb (acquisition): the act is created by Allah, and the human acquires it.

Key Terms
تأثير
قدرتنا
أفعال اختيارية
مخلوقة
ما تعملون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah creates our actions, then humans have no free will and are not responsible.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that although Allah creates the act, humans acquire it through intention (kasb), which is the basis of moral responsibility. The passage does not discuss kasb but lays the foundation that the act is created, not that humans are coerced.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of human power in voluntary actions?
A) It creates the action
B) It has no effect; Allah creates the action and the power
C) It causes the action with Allah's assistance
D) It only influences some actions

Key Takeaway
All human voluntary actions are directly created by Allah, and human power has no creative effect, but humans acquire these actions and are accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every movement you make, even the decision to stand or sit, is created directly by Allah without any effective power from you, how would your sense of reliance on Him change today? Would you still worry about your own planning and effort as if they were independent?
?
Do you actually live as if your actions are created by you, not by Allah? When you succeed, do you credit your own ability? When you fail, do you blame yourself? How does this verse (Quran 37:96) force you to reconsider where you place your trust?
?
If your power itself is a creature of Allah, then what right do you have to be arrogant about any accomplishment? Will you today, before sleeping, ask Allah to forgive the moments you acted as though you were the creator of your own deeds?

Ruling on Jabriyya and Qadariyya
Author rules on Jabriyya and Qadariyya sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Jabriyya are considered disbelievers and why the Qadariyya are not, according to the Ash'ari perspective.

Core Idea
Extreme compulsion nullifies religious obligation; Qadariyya's error is less severe.

This lesson examines the theological rulings on two extreme sects: the Jabriyya, who deny human agency entirely, and the Qadariyya, who overemphasize it. The author clarifies why the former are outright disbelievers while the latter, despite their error, are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the analogy of the thread and the mention of the two sects.
Pay attention to the phrase 'باللأم' which may need verification.
2
Identify the two sects and their positions
Locate where the author describes the Qadariyya belief (effect of originated power) and the Jabriyya belief (negation of choice).
3
Understand the reasoning for each ruling
The author gives a reason for the Jabriyya being kuffar (negation of taklif) and for the Qadariyya not being kuffar (they still affirm Allah's creation of power and will).
Consider why the author says 'the correct view is their non-disbelief'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات. كالخيط المُعَلِّقِ في الهواء، تميله الرياح بلا اختيار له في شَيْءٍ أَصْلًا، وقولُ القَدَريَةِ بتأثير القدرة الحادثة في الأفعال على طبق إرادةِ العَبدِ. والجبرية كفار قطعا، لأنَّ مذهبهم ينفي التكليف الذي جاءَ بِهِ الرُّسُلُ باللأم [؟]، وفي كُفْرِ القَدَريَّةِ خِلافُ (۳) ، الأصَحُ عدَمُ كُفْرهم، لأنهم وإن لزمهم إثبات الشريكِ له تعالى ، إلا أنهم لما أثبتوا الله تعالى خلْقَ العَبدِ وقُدْرَتِهِ وإرادتِه صارَ فِعْلُ العَبْدِ في الحقيقة مخلوقا له تعالى. وعُلِمَ أيضًا أنه لا تأثير للأمور العاديّة في الأمور التي اقترنت بها، فلا تأثير للنار في الإحراق، ولا للطعام في الشبَعِ، ولا للماء في الري ولا في إنبات الزرع، ولا للكواكب في إنضاج الفواكه وغيرها، ولا للأفلاكِ في شَيْءٍ مِنَ الأَشْياءِ، ولا للسكين في القطع، ولا لشيء في دفع حرّ أو برد، أو جلبهما، أو غير ذلك لا بالطَّبع ولا بالعِلةِ ولا بقوة أودعها الله فيها، بل التأثير في ذلك كله الله تعالى وحده، بمخض اختياره عندَ وُجود هذه الأشياء.
(۱) أي لقولهم بجبرية العبد مطلقاً لأن الجبر المطلق يترتب عليه نفي التكليف ويكون إرسال الرسل عبثا، والجبرية نسبة إلى «الجبر» الذي هو نفي الفعل حقيقة عن العبد، وهذه هي الجبرية الخالصة كالجهمية، وهم الذين حكم الشيخ بكفرهم، وأما الجبرية المتوسطة فهم الذين يثبتون للعبد قدرة ولكنها غير مؤثرة أصلا، ولا ينفون التكليف، وعلى هذا يكون الأشاعرة من هذا الصنف.
(٢) أي القائلين من المعتزلة بالقدر، وهم الذين يثبتون القدر للعبد وينفونه عن الله عز وجل.
(۳) هذا ليس من كل وجه وإلا لحكم بكفرهم اتفاقا، ولكن إثبات الشريك من حيث تأثير القدرة الحادثة.
(٤) أي القدرة التي يوجد بها العبد أفعال نفسه الاختيارية ليست ذاتية منه بل هي مخلوقة لله .
(٥) الأمور العادية هي التي يجري فيها الربط بين أمرين كالنار والإحراق، إلا أن هذا الربط يصح في العقل تخلفه، وكل ما دلت عليه العادة هو الاقتران بين الأمرين، أما تأثير أحدهما في الآخر فلا دلالة للعادة عليه أصلا، ولما ثبت دليل وحدانية الأفعال علمنا أنه لا تأثير لأحد سواه تعالى في فعل من الأفعال ألبتة.
The first section: Divine matters. Like the thread hanging in the air, the winds move it with no choice for it in anything at all, and the statement of the Qadariyya about the effect of the originated power in actions according to the slave's will. The Jabriyya are disbelievers decisively, because their doctrine negates the obligation (taklif) with which the messengers came with the Lam [?]. And concerning the disbelief of the Qadariyya there is disagreement (3), the correct view is their non-disbelief, because even though it necessitates for them the affirmation of a partner to Him exalted, yet since they affirmed that Allah creates the slave's power and will, the slave's action in reality becomes created by Him exalted. And it is also known that customary things have no effect in the things with which they are conjoined: fire has no effect in burning, nor food in satiety, nor water in quenching thirst nor in causing plants to grow, nor planets in ripening fruits and other things, nor spheres in anything, nor the knife in cutting, nor anything in repelling heat or cold or bringing them, or anything else — neither by nature (tab‘) nor by causality (‘illa) nor by a power that Allah deposited in them. Rather, the effect in all that is solely Allah, by His sheer choice upon the existence of these things. (1) That is, because of their saying of the compulsion of the slave absolutely, for absolute compulsion entails negation of obligation and makes sending messengers futile. Jabriyya is attributed to 'jabr' which is the negation of the act in reality from the slave. These are the pure Jabriyya like the Jahmiyya, and they are those whom the Shaykh judged as disbelievers. As for the intermediate Jabriyya, they are those who affirm a power for the slave but it is not effective at all, and they do not negate obligation. On this, the Ash‘aris are of this type. (2) That is, those among the Mu‘tazila who affirm [human] power, and they affirm power for the slave and negate it from Allah. (3) This is not from every aspect, otherwise they would be judged disbelievers unanimously, but the affirmation of a partner is from the aspect of the effect of the originated power. (4) That is, the power by which the slave performs his voluntary actions is not intrinsic to him but is created by Allah. (5) Customary things are those in which there is a connection between two things like fire and burning, except that this connection is rationally possible to be severed. All that custom indicates is the conjunction between the two things. As for one's effect on the other, custom gives no indication of it at all. And when the proof of the oneness of acts was established, we know that none other than Him has any effect in any act whatsoever.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage begins by dividing the discussion into the first section on Divine matters. It then uses an analogy of a thread hanging in the air moved by winds without any choice, to illustrate the incapacity of created things. The author then addresses two deviant sects: the Jabriyya and the Qadariyya. The Jabriyya, who claim that the slave has no power at all, are declared outright disbelievers (kuffār qaṭ‘an) because their doctrine negates the very concept of religious obligation (taklīf) — if the slave has no choice, then commands and prohibitions are meaningless, and sending messengers would be futile. The author notes that this refers to the extreme Jabriyya (like the Jahmiyya), while the Ash‘arī position of affirming a non-effective power is a milder form. The Qadariyya, who claim that the slave's originated power (qudra ḥāditha) actually affects actions according to the slave's will, are not necessarily disbelievers. Although their view implies affirming a partner with Allah (since they give the slave independent creative power), they still affirm that Allah creates the slave's power and will, so in reality the slave's action is created by Allah. Hence, the correct position is that they are not disbelievers. The passage then establishes a universal principle: customary things have no effect whatsoever on the events associated with them. A long list of examples is given: fire does not burn by itself, food does not satisfy, water does not quench thirst nor grow plants, planets do not ripen fruits, celestial spheres do not affect anything, a knife does not cut, and nothing repels or brings heat or cold. This negation applies whether one attributes effect to nature (ṭab‘), to causal power (‘illa), or to a power deposited by Allah in the thing. Rather, the real and sole agent is Allah Ta‘ālā alone, by His sheer will, at the time of the existence of these things. The footnotes clarify: the 'customary things' (umūr ‘ādiyya) are correlations that can be severed in the mind; custom indicates only conjunction, not causation. The proof of divine unity of acts leads to the conclusion that none other than Allah has any effect.

Key Terms
الجبرية
القدرية
التكليف
القدرة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position of affirming a non-effective power is the same as the Jabriyya's total negation of human agency.
Correction: The author distinguishes between extreme Jabriyya (kufr) and intermediate Jabriyya (like Ash'aris) who affirm a power but it is not effective, and they do not negate obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the Jabriyya considered disbelievers?
A) They negate religious obligation (taklif).
B) They affirm a partner with Allah.
C) They deny the messengers.
D) They affirm human power.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya are disbelievers because they negate religious obligation, while the Qadariyya, despite their error in affirming effective human power, are not considered disbelievers because they still affirm Allah's creation of that power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you secretly believe that your own 'power' or 'effort' is the decisive factor, forgetting that Allah creates your ability and will? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that every action you perform is ultimately created by Him?
?
The Jabriyya's error was to deny your moral responsibility before Allah. Have you ever used the belief in predestination as an excuse to avoid obeying a difficult command or to justify a sin? What would it look like to fully embrace both Allah's decree and your personal accountability?
?
If you truly believed that your every action is a creation of Allah, how would that change the way you approach repentance, gratitude, and reliance on Him? Take a moment to thank Allah for creating even your ability to think and choose.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Negation of Natural Causality
Author negates causal efficacy of created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that only Allah has true causal efficacy, and that customary correlations are not necessary.

Core Idea
Customary things have no real effect; only Allah acts.

This lesson presents the Ash'ari principle that nothing in creation has inherent causal power. Fire does not burn by its nature, nor does food satisfy hunger. All effects are from Allah alone, by His will. This is rooted in tawḥīd al-af‘āl (oneness of acts).

Guided Path
1
Read the list of denials
Read the passage and notice the long list of examples where effect is denied: fire, food, water, planets, etc.
These are customary correlations that people assume are causal.
2
Understand the three modes of false attribution
The passage negates effect whether by nature (ṭab‘), by causality (‘illa), or by a power deposited by Allah.
This covers all possible naturalistic explanations.
3
Affirm Allah as the sole agent
The passage concludes that only Allah has effect by His sheer choice at the time of these things' existence.
This is the Ash'ari positive doctrine of tawḥīd al-af‘āl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات. كالخيط المُعَلِّقِ في الهواء، تميله الرياح بلا اختيار له في شَيْءٍ أَصْلًا، وقولُ القَدَريَةِ بتأثير القدرة الحادثة في الأفعال على طبق إرادةِ العَبدِ. والجبرية كفار قطعا، لأنَّ مذهبهم ينفي التكليف الذي جاءَ بِهِ الرُّسُلُ باللأم [؟]، وفي كُفْرِ القَدَريَّةِ خِلافُ (۳) ، الأصَحُ عدَمُ كُفْرهم، لأنهم وإن لزمهم إثبات الشريكِ له تعالى ، إلا أنهم لما أثبتوا الله تعالى خلْقَ العَبدِ وقُدْرَتِهِ وإرادتِه صارَ فِعْلُ العَبْدِ في الحقيقة مخلوقا له تعالى. وعُلِمَ أيضًا أنه لا تأثير للأمور العاديّة في الأمور التي اقترنت بها، فلا تأثير للنار في الإحراق، ولا للطعام في الشبَعِ، ولا للماء في الري ولا في إنبات الزرع، ولا للكواكب في إنضاج الفواكه وغيرها، ولا للأفلاكِ في شَيْءٍ مِنَ الأَشْياءِ، ولا للسكين في القطع، ولا لشيء في دفع حرّ أو برد، أو جلبهما، أو غير ذلك لا بالطَّبع ولا بالعِلةِ ولا بقوة أودعها الله فيها، بل التأثير في ذلك كله الله تعالى وحده، بمخض اختياره عندَ وُجود هذه الأشياء.
(۱) أي لقولهم بجبرية العبد مطلقاً لأن الجبر المطلق يترتب عليه نفي التكليف ويكون إرسال الرسل عبثا، والجبرية نسبة إلى «الجبر» الذي هو نفي الفعل حقيقة عن العبد، وهذه هي الجبرية الخالصة كالجهمية، وهم الذين حكم الشيخ بكفرهم، وأما الجبرية المتوسطة فهم الذين يثبتون للعبد قدرة ولكنها غير مؤثرة أصلا، ولا ينفون التكليف، وعلى هذا يكون الأشاعرة من هذا الصنف.
(٢) أي القائلين من المعتزلة بالقدر، وهم الذين يثبتون القدر للعبد وينفونه عن الله عز وجل.
(۳) هذا ليس من كل وجه وإلا لحكم بكفرهم اتفاقا، ولكن إثبات الشريك من حيث تأثير القدرة الحادثة.
(٤) أي القدرة التي يوجد بها العبد أفعال نفسه الاختيارية ليست ذاتية منه بل هي مخلوقة لله .
(٥) الأمور العادية هي التي يجري فيها الربط بين أمرين كالنار والإحراق، إلا أن هذا الربط يصح في العقل تخلفه، وكل ما دلت عليه العادة هو الاقتران بين الأمرين، أما تأثير أحدهما في الآخر فلا دلالة للعادة عليه أصلا، ولما ثبت دليل وحدانية الأفعال علمنا أنه لا تأثير لأحد سواه تعالى في فعل من الأفعال ألبتة.
The first section: Divine matters. Like the thread hanging in the air, the winds move it with no choice for it in anything at all, and the statement of the Qadariyya about the effect of the originated power in actions according to the slave's will. The Jabriyya are disbelievers decisively, because their doctrine negates the obligation (taklif) with which the messengers came with the Lam [?]. And concerning the disbelief of the Qadariyya there is disagreement (3), the correct view is their non-disbelief, because even though it necessitates for them the affirmation of a partner to Him exalted, yet since they affirmed that Allah creates the slave's power and will, the slave's action in reality becomes created by Him exalted. Jabriyya is attributed to 'jabr' which is the negation of the act in reality from the slave. These are the pure Jabriyya like the Jahmiyya, and they are those whom the Shaykh judged as disbelievers. (2) That is, those among the Mu‘tazila who affirm [human] power, and they affirm power for the slave and negate it from Allah. (3) This is not from every aspect, otherwise they would be judged disbelievers unanimously, but the affirmation of a partner is from the aspect of the effect of the originated power. (4) That is, the power by which the slave performs his voluntary actions is not intrinsic to him but is created by Allah. Commentary
This passage begins by dividing the discussion into the first section on Divine matters. It then uses an analogy of a thread hanging in the air moved by winds without any choice, to illustrate the incapacity of created things. The author then addresses two deviant sects: the Jabriyya and the Qadariyya. The Jabriyya, who claim that the slave has no power at all, are declared outright disbelievers (kuffār qaṭ‘an) because their doctrine negates the very concept of religious obligation (taklīf) — if the slave has no choice, then commands and prohibitions are meaningless, and sending messengers would be futile. The author notes that this refers to the extreme Jabriyya (like the Jahmiyya), while the Ash‘arī position of affirming a non-effective power is a milder form. The Qadariyya, who claim that the slave's originated power (qudra ḥāditha) actually affects actions according to the slave's will, are not necessarily disbelievers. Although their view implies affirming a partner with Allah (since they give the slave independent creative power), they still affirm that Allah creates the slave's power and will, so in reality the slave's action is created by Allah. Hence, the correct position is that they are not disbelievers. The passage then establishes a universal principle: customary things have no effect whatsoever on the events associated with them. A long list of examples is given: fire does not burn by itself, food does not satisfy, water does not quench thirst nor grow plants, planets do not ripen fruits, celestial spheres do not affect anything, a knife does not cut, and nothing repels or brings heat or cold. This negation applies whether one attributes effect to nature (ṭab‘), to causal power (‘illa), or to a power deposited by Allah in the thing. Rather, the real and sole agent is Allah Ta‘ālā alone, by His sheer will, at the time of the existence of these things. The footnotes clarify: the 'customary things' (umūr ‘ādiyya) are correlations that can be severed in the mind; custom indicates only conjunction, not causation. The proof of divine unity of acts leads to the conclusion that none other than Allah has any effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari denial of causality means actions are random or without order.
Correction: The passage affirms that customary conjunctions exist; they are just not necessary. The order is from Allah's habit (sunna).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true source of the effect in burning?
A) The fire's nature (ṭab‘).
B) Allah's will.
C) The knife's sharpness.
D) The power deposited in the fire.

Key Takeaway
No created thing has inherent causal power; all effects are directly from Allah by His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your heart: when you take medicine, do you rely on the medicine or on Allah? How can you shift your trust?
?

?
If all effects are directly from Allah, then what about your own achievements? Do you attribute your successes to your own abilities or to Allah? Repent from any sense of self-sufficiency. Write down one success you recently became proud of and consciously attribute it to Allah alone.

The Rational Proof for Negative Attributes
Dardeer proves negative attributes via a conditional argument against temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah must be described with negative attributes such as eternity, everlastingness, and others, and how their absence would imply temporality.

Core Idea
If Allah lacked negative attributes, He would be temporal—impossible for the Necessary Being.

This lesson presents the proof for Allah's negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) as expounded by Imam Dardeer. Using a conditional argument, the text demonstrates that the absence of each negative attribute would entail temporality (huduth), which is impossible for Allah. The proof is concise but powerful, grounding these attributes in pure reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, including the poem line and commentary. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If He were not qualified... then His temporality would be necessary.'
2
Identify the negative attributes listed
The commentary enumerates five negative attributes: eternal (qadim), everlasting (baqi), not similar to temporal things, self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi), and one (wahid). Note each one.
3
Examine the proof for everlastingness
The text gives a detailed proof for baqa': if Allah were not everlasting, He could cease to exist, but since He is eternal, non-existence is impossible; otherwise a specificator would be needed, implying temporality. Trace this logic step by step.
This proof exemplifies how each negative attribute is rationally necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان الصفات
۲۸ - لو لم يكُنْ مُتَّصِفًا بها لزِمَ * حُدوتُهُ وهُوَ مُحالٌ، فَاسْتَقِمْ -
ثُمَّ أَشار إلى بُرهانِ الصِّفاتِ السلبية إجمالًا بِقَولِه: (لو لم يكُن) أي: إنّها وجَبَ اتَّصافه بالصِّفاتِ السلبية لأنه لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُنصف السلبية بها بأن كان غير قديم أو باق، أو كان مُماثلا للحوادث، أو غَيْرَ قائم بنَفْسِه، أو غير واحدٍ فيها مرَّ، (الزم حدوثه) تعالى عن ذلك. أما القِدَمُ فظاهر. وأما البقاء فلأنه لو لم يكُن مُتَصِفًا به لَمْ يَكُنْ قديا، لأنَّ مَنْ ثَبَتَ قِدَمُه استَحالَ عَدَمُه، وإلا لكانَ جاءَ العدم، فيحتاجُ إلى مُرَجّح، وكُلِّ مُحْتاج إلى مُرَجّح حادِثٌ.
Proof of the Attributes. 28. If He were not qualified with them, His temporality would be necessary, and that is impossible, so be steadfast. Then he indicated, may Allah forgive him, to the proof of the negative attributes summarily by his saying: (If He were not) — that is, indeed it is necessary that He be qualified with the negative attributes, because if He were not qualified with the negative attributes, such as being not eternal or everlasting, or being similar to temporal things, or not self-subsisting, or not one in the way mentioned, then His temporality would be necessary, exalted is He above that. As for eternity, it is apparent. As for everlastingness, because if He were not qualified with it, He would not be eternal, since whoever's eternity is established, His non-existence is impossible; otherwise non-existence would occur, and then He would need a specificator, and everything needing a specificator is temporal.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the negative attributes of Allah (sifat salbiyya). It begins with a line from the poem: 'If He were not qualified with them, His temporality would be necessary, and that is impossible, so be steadfast.' The commentary explains that this conditional argument establishes that Allah must be described with these negative attributes because otherwise He would be temporal (hadith), which is impossible. The negative attributes mentioned include: being eternal (qadim), everlasting (baqi), not similar to temporal things, self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi), and one (wahid). The proof for each is sketched. Eternity (qidam) is straightforward: if He were not eternal, He would be temporal. The proof for everlastingness (baqa') is elaborated: if Allah were not everlasting, He would not be eternal, because one whose eternity is established cannot cease to exist; if non-existence were possible, a specificator would be needed to bring it about, and anything needing a specificator is temporal. Therefore, Allah must be everlasting. The overall argument is that the absence of any of these negative attributes would entail temporality, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Misconception: Some think these attributes are arbitrarily chosen or are derived solely from revelation.
Correction: The proof shows they are rationally necessary: denying any leads to the absurdity of attributing temporality to Allah.

Key Takeaway
Allah's negative attributes are rationally necessary to negate temporality and its implications.

The Proof for Eternity (Qidam)
Proof for qidam via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of eternity (qidam) is proven through a reductio ad absurdum argument that avoids circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Eternity is proven by showing that its opposite leads to absurdity.

Imam Dardeer begins the proof for qidam by stating that it is 'apparent'. He then provides a more elaborate argument in the footnote: if God were not eternal, He would be originated, leading to an infinite chain of causes or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, God must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the main text says it is 'apparent', but the footnote provides the full argument.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The argument assumes the opposite (that God is not eternal) and shows that it leads to an absurd consequence, so the opposite must be false.
3
Understand why circularity and infinite regress are impossible
The proof shows that if God were originated, we would need an infinite chain of causes or a circular cause. Both are rationally impossible, so God cannot be originated.
This is a standard argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا القِدَمُ فظاهِرٌ (٣). وأمَّا البَقاءُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفًا بِهِ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَديمًا، لأنَّ مَنْ ثَبَتَ قِدَمُهُ اسْتَحالَ عَدَمُهُ، وإلَّا لَكانَ جائِزَ العَدَمِ، فيَحْتاجُ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ، وكُلُّ مُحْتاجٍ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ حادِثٌ.
(٣) أي لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَديمًا لَكانَ حادِثًا، وَلَوْ كانَ حادِثًا لافْتَقَرَ إلى مُحْدِثٍ وهَكَذا، فَيَلْزَمُ إمَّا الدَّوْرُ وإمَّا التَّسَلْسُلُ وكِلاهُما باطِلٌ، فَبَطَلَ ما أَدَّى إلَيْهِما وهو كَوْنُهُ تَعالى حادِثًا، فَثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو كَوْنُهُ قَديمًا وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٤) إشارَةٌ إلى قياسٍ اسْتِثْنائِيٍّ حُذِفَتْ صُغْراهُ، وتَقْريرُهُ: هكذا المولى مُتَّصِفٌ بِالبَقاءِ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفًا بِهِ لَكانَ جائِزَ القِدَمِ، وإذا كانَ جائِزَ القِدَمِ احْتاجَ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ، وكُلُّ مُحْتاجٍ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ حادِثٌ، لكنَّ كَوْنَهُ جائِزَ القِدَمِ مُحالٌ لِما تَقَدَّمَ مِن وُجوبِ وُجودِهِ تَعالى إلخ.
As for eternity, it is apparent. (3) As for everlastingness, it is because if He were not described with it, He would not be eternal, for whoever's eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible; otherwise he would be possible of non-existence, and would need a preponderator, and everything in need of a preponderator is originated. (3) That is: if He were not eternal, He would be originated, and if He were originated, He would need an originator, and so on; then either circularity or infinite regress would necessarily follow, and both are false; therefore what led to them – namely His being originated – is false, so its opposite – His being eternal – is established, and that is the desired conclusion. (4) This is a reference to an exceptional syllogism whose minor premise has been omitted. Its formulation: likewise, the Master is described with everlastingness, because if He were not described with it, He would be possible of eternity; and if He were possible of eternity, He would need a preponderator, and everything in need of a preponderator is originated; but His being possible of eternity is impossible due to what has preceded of the necessity of His existence, etc.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage proves two attributes: qidam (eternity without beginning) and baqā' (everlastingness without end). For qidam, the main text simply says 'it is apparent,' and the footnote (3) elaborates with a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not eternal, He would be originated; if originated, He would need an originator; this leads to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible. Therefore, the negation is false, so eternity is established. For baqā', the proof is that if God were not described with everlastingness, then His eternal existence would be merely possible (jā'iz al-qidam), meaning He could cease to exist. But if something is possible of non-existence, it requires a preponderator (murajjih) to bring it into existence or sustain it; whatever requires a preponderator is originated (ḥādith). However, God's existence is necessary (wājib al-wujūd), not possible, so the assumption is false. Thus, He must be described with everlastingness. Both proofs rely on the principle that the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything external, nor can He be subject to non-existence.

Key Terms
قدم
حادث
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity is self-evident and requires no proof.
Correction: While Imam Dardeer states it is 'apparent,' he still provides a detailed reductio ad absurdum to show that denying eternity leads to impossible consequences.

Checkpoint
What two logical problems does the proof for qidam argue would follow if God were not eternal?
A) Dawr (circularity) and tasalsul (infinite regress)
B) Tajsim (anthropomorphism) and tashbih (comparison)
C) 'Adam (non-existence) and huduth (origination)
D) Tawhid (unity) and tawakkul (trust)

Key Takeaway
The attribute of qidam is proven by showing that its negation leads to the impossible consequences of circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is eternal and uncaused, why do you sometimes act as if your existence is self-sufficient or independent of Him?
?
The argument proves that infinite regress is absurd. Have you ever allowed your worries about the future to spiral into an endless chain of 'what ifs'? How does trusting in the Eternal One cut that chain?
?
Believing in qidam means Allah alone has no beginning. What would change in your daily life if you constantly reminded yourself that everything else, including you, is originated and dependent on Him?

The Proof for Everlastingness (Baqa)
Proof for baqa via the impossibility of contingent eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of everlastingness (baqa) is proven by showing that God's existence is necessary and cannot be subject to non-existence.

Core Idea
Everlastingness is proven because God's eternity cannot be merely possible; it must be necessary.

Having established that God is eternal (qidam), Imam Dardeer now proves that this eternity is not temporary but perpetual. The argument is that if God were not described with baqa, then His eternity would be merely possible (jā'iz al-qidam), meaning He could cease to exist. But anything that can cease to exist requires a preponderator to keep it in existence, and whatever depends on a preponderator is originated. Since God's existence is necessary, He cannot be possible of non-existence, so He must be everlasting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the conditional structure in the main text and the syllogism in the footnote.
2
Identify the conditional chain
The argument goes: if no baqa, then jā'iz al-qidam; if jā'iz al-qidam, then needs murajjih; if needs murajjih, then originated. So no baqa leads to originated, which contradicts necessary existence.
The footnote formalizes this as an exceptional syllogism.
3
Understand why jā'iz al-qidam is impossible
God's existence is necessary (wājib al-wujūd); it cannot be merely possible. Therefore, He must have baqa.
This connects to the earlier proof of necessary existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا القِدَمُ فظاهِرٌ (٣). وأمَّا البَقاءُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفًا بِهِ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَديمًا، لأنَّ مَنْ ثَبَتَ قِدَمُهُ اسْتَحالَ عَدَمُهُ، وإلَّا لَكانَ جائِزَ العَدَمِ، فيَحْتاجُ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ، وكُلُّ مُحْتاجٍ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ حادِثٌ.
(٣) أي لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَديمًا لَكانَ حادِثًا، وَلَوْ كانَ حادِثًا لافْتَقَرَ إلى مُحْدِثٍ وهَكَذا، فَيَلْزَمُ إمَّا الدَّوْرُ وإمَّا التَّسَلْسُلُ وكِلاهُما باطِلٌ، فَبَطَلَ ما أَدَّى إلَيْهِما وهو كَوْنُهُ تَعالى حادِثًا، فَثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو كَوْنُهُ قَديمًا وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٤) إشارَةٌ إلى قياسٍ اسْتِثْنائِيٍّ حُذِفَتْ صُغْراهُ، وتَقْريرُهُ: هكذا المولى مُتَّصِفٌ بِالبَقاءِ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُتَّصِفًا بِهِ لَكانَ جائِزَ القِدَمِ، وإذا كانَ جائِزَ القِدَمِ احْتاجَ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ، وكُلُّ مُحْتاجٍ إلى مُرَجِّحٍ حادِثٌ، لكنَّ كَوْنَهُ جائِزَ القِدَمِ مُحالٌ لِما تَقَدَّمَ مِن وُجوبِ وُجودِهِ تَعالى إلخ.
As for eternity, it is apparent. (3) As for everlastingness, it is because if He were not described with it, He would not be eternal, for whoever's eternity is established, his non-existence is impossible; otherwise he would be possible of non-existence, and would need a preponderator, and everything in need of a preponderator is originated. (3) That is: if He were not eternal, He would be originated, and if He were originated, He would need an originator, and so on; then either circularity or infinite regress would necessarily follow, and both are false; therefore what led to them – namely His being originated – is false, so its opposite – His being eternal – is established, and that is the desired conclusion. (4) This is a reference to an exceptional syllogism whose minor premise has been omitted. Its formulation: likewise, the Master is described with everlastingness, because if He were not described with it, He would be possible of eternity; and if He were possible of eternity, He would need a preponderator, and everything in need of a preponderator is originated; but His being possible of eternity is impossible due to what has preceded of the necessity of His existence, etc.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage proves two attributes: qidam (eternity without beginning) and baqā' (everlastingness without end). For qidam, the main text simply says 'it is apparent,' and the footnote (3) elaborates with a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not eternal, He would be originated; if originated, He would need an originator; this leads to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible. Therefore, the negation is false, so eternity is established. For baqā', the proof is that if God were not described with everlastingness, then His eternal existence would be merely possible (jā'iz al-qidam), meaning He could cease to exist. But if something is possible of non-existence, it requires a preponderator (murajjih) to bring it into existence or sustain it; whatever requires a preponderator is originated (ḥādith). However, God's existence is necessary (wājib al-wujūd), not possible, so the assumption is false. Thus, He must be described with everlastingness. Both proofs rely on the principle that the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything external, nor can He be subject to non-existence.

Correction: The proof shows that even if something is eternal, it could still be possible for it to cease unless its existence is necessary. Baqa is not automatically implied by qidam; it requires a separate argument based on the necessity of existence.

Proving God's Self-Subsistence
Imam Dardeer proves God's self-subsistence via elimination of alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God must be self-subsistent (qiyām bi nafs) and why alternatives—being an accident or a quality—are impossible.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence is necessary because God cannot depend on a substrate or specifier.

Imam Dardeer now proves the attribute of self-subsistence by eliminating every alternative. If God were not self-subsistent, He would have to be either an accident (needing a substrate) or an eternal quality (needing a subject). Both lead to absurdities, especially the impossibility of a quality possessing other qualities. Follow the logical steps in the text and see how the proof is structured as a conditional syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the main text (the matn) first, then the commentary (the sharh) to see the full syllogistic reasoning.
The passage uses technical terms like 'arad' and 'sifa'—note their definitions.
2
Identify the two alternatives to self-subsistence
The text says if not self-subsistent, then either accident (arad) or eternal quality (sifa). Understand why each is impossible for God.
Accidents are dependent and originated; qualities need a subject.
3
Understand the syllogism for 'not a quality'
Follow the second syllogism: if God were a quality, He could not be described with attributes of meanings (like knowledge), but He is, so He is not a quality.
This is based on the principle that a quality does not accept another quality.
4
Reach the conclusion: self-subsistence is necessary
Since both alternatives are false, the opposite—self-subsistence—must be true. This is the desired result.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ (١) فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ قامَ بِغَيْرِهِ لَكانَ عَرَضًا، وقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ بَيانُ الأَعْراضِ، أو كانَ صِفَةً قَديمَةً قائِمَةً بِمَوْصوفِها (٣)، فَيَلْزَمُ أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ بِصِفاتِ المَعاني؛ لِمَا مَرَّ (٣)، وهو باطِلٌ.
(١) وأمَّا القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ إلخ. تَقْريرُ الدَّليلِ: المولى يَجِبُ لَهُ القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَقُمْ بِنَفْسِهِ لَكانَ عَرَضًا أو صِفَةً، وإذا كانَ كَذلِكَ احْتاجَ إلى المَحَلِّ أو إلى المُخَصِّصِ، لكنَّ كَوْنَهُ عَرَضًا أو صِفَةً مُحالٌ فَبَطَلَ مَلْزومُهُ وهو «لَمْ يَقُمْ بِنَفْسِهِ» فَثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو وُجوبُ القِيامِ بِالنَّفْسِ وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٢) وهو إشارَةٌ إلى قياسٍ اسْتِثْنائِيٍّ؛ وتَقْريرُهُ: المولى لَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ صِفَةً لَزِمَ أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ بِصِفاتِ المَعاني، واللازِمُ وهو أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ باطِلٌ، وإذا بَطَلَ اللازِمُ بَطَلَ المَلْزومُ وهو كَوْنُهُ صِفَةً، وإذا بَطَلَ المَلْزومُ ثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو كَوْنُهُ لَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ، وإذا انْتَفى كَوْنُهُ صِفَةً ثَبَتَ أنَّهُ ذاتٌ وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٣) «لِمَا مَرَّ» أي مِن أنَّ الصِّفَةَ لا تَقْبَلُ صِفَةً أُخْرى.
As for self-subsistence (1): because if He subsisted through another, He would be an accident, and the explanation of accidents has preceded; or He would be an eternal quality subsisting in its subject (3), then it would necessarily follow that He is not described with the attributes of meanings, due to what has passed (3), and that is false. (1) As for self-subsistence, etc. The formulation of the proof: the Master must have self-subsistence, because if He did not subsist by Himself, He would be an accident or a quality; and if that were the case, He would need a substrate (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); but His being an accident or a quality is impossible; therefore its antecedent – 'He did not subsist by Himself' – is false, so its opposite – the necessity of self-subsistence – is established, and that is the desired conclusion. (2) This is a reference to an exceptional syllogism; its formulation: the Master is not a quality, because if He were a quality, it would necessarily follow that He is not described with the attributes of meanings, and the necessary consequence – that He is not described – is false; so when the consequence is false, the antecedent – His being a quality – is false; and when the antecedent is false, its opposite – that He is not a quality – is established; and when His being a quality is negated, it is established that He is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt), and that is the desired conclusion. (3) 'Due to what has passed' means that a quality does not accept another quality.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of qiyām bi nafs (self-subsistence). The main text argues that if God were not self-subsistent, He would either be an accident (ʿaraḍ) – which subsists in a substrate – or an eternal quality (ṣifa) subsisting in a subject. Both possibilities are impossible for God. The first alternative (accident) is impossible because accidents are originated and dependent; the second alternative (eternal quality) would entail that God cannot be described with the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) – such as knowledge, power, will – because a quality cannot support another quality (as explained in footnote 3). Since God does possess those attributes, He cannot be a quality. Therefore, He must be self-subsistent. The footnotes provide full formal syllogisms: the first (1) is a conditional proof that if not self-subsistent, then accident or quality, both impossible, so self-subsistence is necessary. The second (2) is a separate syllogism proving that God is not a quality at all, using the impossibility of a quality being described with other qualities. The conclusion is that God is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt).

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس
عرض
صفة
محل
مخصص
ذات
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence merely means God is independent of creation.
Correction: Self-subsistence (qiyām bi nafs) specifically means God does not need a substrate (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); it is a technical term in kalām about ontological independence from a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot God be a quality (ṣifa)?
A) Because qualities are temporal
B) Because a quality cannot be described with other qualities (attributes of meanings)
C) Because qualities are multiple
D) Because qualities are dependent on a subject

Key Takeaway
God must be self-subsistent because the alternatives—being an accident or a quality—lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live with the certainty that God needs nothing from you? How does that freedom affect the way you worship and rely on Him?
?
When you feel pressured to prove your worth to others, are you forgetting that God is self-sufficient and needs no validation? How can you shift your focus from human approval to divine acceptance?
?
If God were dependent on a substrate or specifier, He would be limited. Do you ever act as if God needs your praise or obedience for His own benefit? How can you purify your intention to worship only because He is worthy, not because He needs it?

The Core Argument for God's Oneness
Imam Dardeer states the primary premise for proving oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of a peer for Allah would necessitate incapability and temporality, which is impossible for the eternal God.

Core Idea
No equal can exist with Allah, else both would be incapable and originated.

Imam Dardeer begins the proof for God's oneness with a simple conditional: if there were a peer, there would be incapability, and every incapable thing is originated. Since Allah is eternal and free from deficiency, this impossibility eliminates any possibility of a partner. This lesson unpacks that powerful premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The phrase 'due to what has passed' refers to earlier proofs about incapability.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on nazir (peer), 'ajz (incapability), and hadith (originated). Understand why 'ajz implies hadath (temporality).
In kalam, everything originated is preceded by non-existence.
3
Reflect on the implication
If God had a peer, both would be incapable. Since incapability is a deficiency incompatible with divinity, the assumption is false.
This argument is concise yet decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الوَحْدانِيَّةُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ لَهُ نَظيرٌ في ذاتِهِ أو صِفاتِهِ لِلَزِمَ العَجْزُ، لِمَا مَرَّ ( )، وكُلُّ عاجِزٍ حادِثٌ.
(ه) هذا دَليلُ المُلازَمَةِ، وهذا البُرْهانُ يُسَمَّى بُرْهانَ التَّوارُدِ لِتَوارُدِ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على أثَرٍ واحِدٍ، وتَقْريرُهُ عِنْدَ اتِّفاقِهِما على إيجادِ شَيْءٍ مُعَيَّنٍ: تَوارُدُ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على شَيْءٍ واحِدٍ لِعُمومِ تَعَلُّقِهِما لَوْ تَعَدَّدَ الإلَهُ لَزِمَ بِكُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ، وتَوارُدُهُما يُؤَدِّي إلى عَدَمِ وُجودِهِ، لأنَّهُ إمَّا أنْ يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا فَيَلْزَمُ تَحْصيلُ الحاصِلِ، يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا بأنْ وُجِدَ بِأحَدِهِما فَيَلْزَمُ عَجْزُ الآخَرِ لاِنْعِقادِ الماثِلَةِ بَيْنَهُما فَلا يُمْكِنُ إيجادُهُ بِواحِدَةٍ مِنَ القُدْرَتَيْنِ. وإذا كانَ هذا عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ فَعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ مِن بابِ أَوْلى. فالتَّعَدُّدُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَدَمِ إيجادِ الأَشْياءِ عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ وعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ، والمُشاهَدُ وُجودُ الأَشْياءِ، فَدَلَّتْ مُشاهَدَةُ وُجودِها على عَدَمِ التَّعَدُّدِ وهو المَطْلوبُ. [السَّباعِي، ص ٣١٧] وهو مُحالٌ، وإمَّا ألا
As for oneness: because if He had an equal in His essence or attributes, incapability would necessarily follow, due to what has passed ( ), and every incapable thing is originated. (5) This is the proof of the implication, and this proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud) due to the mutual hindrance of their two powers upon one effect. Its formulation when they agree on bringing into existence a specific thing: their two powers converge upon one thing due to the universality of their attachment; if there were multiple gods, it would apply to every possible thing. Their convergence leads to its non-existence, because either it would be brought into existence by both together, which would entail the obtaining of the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); or it would be brought into existence by one of them, which would entail the incapability of the other due to the obstacle (māthila) between them, so it could not be brought into existence by either power. If this is the case when they agree, then when they disagree it is even more so. Therefore multiplicity necessitates the non-bringing into existence of things both when they agree and when they disagree. But the existence of things is observed, so the observation of their existence indicates the absence of multiplicity, and that is the desired conclusion. [al-Saba'i, p. 317] And that is impossible, or else...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of waḥdāniyya (oneness) – that God is one without partner. The main text argues that if God had a peer (nazīr) in essence or attributes, it would necessitate incapability (ʿajz) in each, and everything incapable is originated, which is impossible for God. The footnote (5) provides the classical 'proof of mutual hindrance' (burhān al-tawārud) often used in kalam. The logic is: if there were two gods, they would either agree or disagree on creating a specific thing. If they agree, both powers would converge on one effect; either both create it together (which is impossible because it would be a duplication of the already existent), or one creates it while the other is incapable, proving its incapability. If they disagree, one wants to create and the other wants to prevent it, leading to conflict and non-existence of the thing. Since we observe the existence of things, the assumption of multiple gods is false. Therefore, God is necessarily one. The phrase 'each incapable is originated' establishes that incapability entails temporality, which cannot apply to the eternal God.

Key Terms
وحدانية
نظير
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple gods could coexist if they cooperate.
Correction: The argument shows that even cooperation leads to incapability, because each power would depend on the other, and each would be incapable of independent action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the existence of a peer for Allah entail?
A) Wisdom
B) Incapability
C) Power
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a peer for Allah is negated because it would lead to incapability and temporality, contradicting His eternal perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart, treat a person, a possession, or a status as an 'equal' to Allah in the sense that you believe they can bring you benefit or harm independent of Him? How does the logical impossibility of a peer challenge that hidden assumption? This week, when you feel fear or hope tied to something other than Allah, pause and recite the argument from this lesson: 'If there were an equal, incapability would follow.' Will you commit to that practice?
?
The proof shows that any supposed partner would make Allah 'incapable' (عاجز). Where in your life do you act as if Allah's power is limited by circumstances, people, or your own mistakes? How does acknowledging His absolute oneness strip those limitations away? Write down one area of worry and place it under the statement 'لا نظير له' — He has no peer.
?
If you truly internalized that 'every incapable thing is originated' and that Allah is neither incapable nor originated, would your view of your own weaknesses change? Do you see your own temporary limitations as a sign that you are a created being dependent on the One without peer? How does that humility reshape your dua today?

The Proof of Mutual Hindrance (Burhān al-Tawārud)
Imam Dardeer presents the detailed proof of mutual hindrance against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed logical proof that multiple gods cannot coexist, based on the impossibility of two powers acting on one thing.

Core Idea
If two gods exist, their powers would hinder each other, leading to non-existence of things.

In this lesson, we dive into the classical proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud). Imam Dardeer explains that if there were two gods with universal power, their powers would converge on every possible thing. Whether they agree or disagree, the result is that nothing could exist. Since we observe existence, there must be only one God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Imagine two gods with equal and universal power. Their powers would apply to every possible thing.
The proof assumes both have complete power over all things.
2
Consider agreement
If they agree on creating a specific thing, either both create it together (impossible duplication) or one creates it while the other is unable (proving incapability).
The term 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (obtaining the obtained) means something that already exists cannot be brought into existence again.
3
Consider disagreement
If one wants to create and the other wants to prevent, conflict leads to non-existence.
Disagreement is even more problematic than agreement.
4
Apply to observed world
Since we see things exist, the assumption of multiple gods must be false.
This is a proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الوَحْدانِيَّةُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ لَهُ نَظيرٌ في ذاتِهِ أو صِفاتِهِ لِلَزِمَ العَجْزُ، لِمَا مَرَّ ( )، وكُلُّ عاجِزٍ حادِثٌ.
(ه) هذا دَليلُ المُلازَمَةِ، وهذا البُرْهانُ يُسَمَّى بُرْهانَ التَّوارُدِ لِتَوارُدِ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على أثَرٍ واحِدٍ، وتَقْريرُهُ عِنْدَ اتِّفاقِهِما على إيجادِ شَيْءٍ مُعَيَّنٍ: تَوارُدُ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على شَيْءٍ واحِدٍ لِعُمومِ تَعَلُّقِهِما لَوْ تَعَدَّدَ الإلَهُ لَزِمَ بِكُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ، وتَوارُدُهُما يُؤَدِّي إلى عَدَمِ وُجودِهِ، لأنَّهُ إمَّا أنْ يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا فَيَلْزَمُ تَحْصيلُ الحاصِلِ، يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا بأنْ وُجِدَ بِأحَدِهِما فَيَلْزَمُ عَجْزُ الآخَرِ لاِنْعِقادِ الماثِلَةِ بَيْنَهُما فَلا يُمْكِنُ إيجادُهُ بِواحِدَةٍ مِنَ القُدْرَتَيْنِ. وإذا كانَ هذا عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ فَعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ مِن بابِ أَوْلى. فالتَّعَدُّدُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَدَمِ إيجادِ الأَشْياءِ عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ وعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ، والمُشاهَدُ وُجودُ الأَشْياءِ، فَدَلَّتْ مُشاهَدَةُ وُجودِها على عَدَمِ التَّعَدُّدِ وهو المَطْلوبُ. [السَّباعِي، ص ٣١٧] وهو مُحالٌ، وإمَّا ألا
As for oneness: because if He had an equal in His essence or attributes, incapability would necessarily follow, due to what has passed ( ), and every incapable thing is originated. (5) This is the proof of the implication, and this proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud) due to the mutual hindrance of their two powers upon one effect. Its formulation when they agree on bringing into existence a specific thing: their two powers converge upon one thing due to the universality of their attachment; if there were multiple gods, it would apply to every possible thing. If this is the case when they agree, then when they disagree it is even more so. Therefore multiplicity necessitates the non-bringing into existence of things both when they agree and when they disagree. But the existence of things is observed, so the observation of their existence indicates the absence of multiplicity, and that is the desired conclusion. [al-Saba'i, p. 317] And that is impossible, or else...

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of waḥdāniyya (oneness) – that God is one without partner. The main text argues that if God had a peer (nazīr) in essence or attributes, it would necessitate incapability (ʿajz) in each, and everything incapable is originated, which is impossible for God. In the proof of mutual hindrance, what does 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' refer to?
A) The creation of something from nothing
B) The duplication of an already existing thing

D) The prevention of an effect

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance demonstrates that multiple gods would lead to the non-existence of all things, contradicting the observed existence, thereby proving God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that multiple gods would lead to chaos and non-existence, how would that affect your trust in the order of the universe? Do you live as if the universe is sustained by a single God, or do you sometimes act as if chance or multiple forces control your life? Commit to recognizing God's sole power in every moment today.
?
The proof shows that agreement between two gods would lead to impossibility. In your own life, when you try to serve multiple masters or pursue conflicting goals, do you experience a similar 'mutual hindrance'? What is one area where you need to submit solely to Allah's will to find coherence?
?
The proof relies on the observable existence of things. Do you ever take the existence of things for granted? How can you use this observation to strengthen your conviction in tawḥīd? Make a habit of reflecting on the order of creation as a direct sign of God's oneness.

Proof of Divine Self-Subsistence
Dardeer proves God's necessity of self-subsistence using an exceptive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof for God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) using an exceptive syllogism, and why the possibilities of God being an accident or attribute are impossible.

Core Idea
God must be self-subsistent because He cannot be an accident or attribute.

This lesson presents a concise logical proof from Imam Dardeer that establishes God's self-subsistence. By examining what would follow if God were not self-subsistent, the argument shows that only self-subsistence is possible. The proof uses an exceptive syllogism, a common tool in Islamic theology, to rule out alternatives. Understanding this argument clarifies why Muslims affirm that God does not depend on anything for His existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if He subsisted by another, He would be an accident or an attribute.'
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two things God would be if He were not self-subsistent: an accident (ʿaraḍ) or an attribute (ṣifa). Understand why each is impossible for God.
An accident is perishable; an attribute cannot receive another attribute.
3
Follow the exceptive syllogism
The commentary explains the logical structure: if God were an attribute, He could not have attributes of meanings. Since He does have them, He is not an attribute. Therefore He is a self (dhāt) and self-subsistent.
This is a qiyās istithnāʾī—a conditional syllogism that eliminates the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القيام بالنفس (۱) فلأنه لو قامَ بغيره لكانَ عرَضًا، وقد تقدَّمَ بيانُ - الأعراض، أو كان صفةً قديمة قائمة بموصوفها (٣) ، فيلزَمُ أنْ لا يتصف بصفات المعاني؛ لما مَرَّ (٣)، وهو باطل. (۱) وأما القيام بالنفس إلخ. تقرير الدليل : المولى يجب له القيام بالنفس، لأنه لو لم يقم بنفسه لكان عرضًا أو صفة، وإذا كان كذلك احتاج إلى المحل أو إلى المخصص، لكن كونه عرضًا أو صفة محال فبطل ملزومه وهو «لم يقم بنفسه إلخ » ، فثبت نقيضه وهو وجوب القيام بنفسه وهو المطلوب. (۲) وهو إشارة إلى قياس استثنائي؛ وتقريره : المولى ليس بصفة، لأنه لو كان صفة لزم أن لا يتصف بصفات المعاني، واللازم وهو أن لا يتصف .. باطل، وإذا بطل اللازم بطل الملزوم وهو كونه صفة، وإذا بطل الملزوم ثبت نقيضه وهو كونه ليس بصفة ، وإذا انتفى كونه صفة ثبت أنه ذات وهو المطلوب. [حاشية السباعي، ص ٩٤] (۳) «لما مر » أي من أن الصفة لا تقبل صفة أخرى. [سباعي، ص ٣١٥]
As for self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs), because if He subsisted by another, He would be an accident, and the explanation of accidents has preceded, or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its described, and then it would necessarily follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), due to what has passed, and that is false. (1) 'As for self-subsistence, etc.' Explanation of the proof: The Master (al-mawlā) must have self-subsistence, because if He were not self-subsistent, He would be an accident or an attribute, and if so, He would need a substrate or a specificator, but His being an accident or attribute is impossible, so its entailer—namely 'He is not self-subsistent, etc.'—is null, thus its opposite is established, which is the obligation of self-subsistence, and that is the required. (2) It is an indication of an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnāʾī); its formulation: The Master is not an attribute, because if He were an attribute, it would necessarily follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings, and the consequent—that He is not described—is false, and if the consequent is null, its antecedent—His being an attribute—is null, and if the antecedent is null, its opposite—that He is not an attribute—is established, and if His being an attribute is negated, it is established that He is a self (dhāt), and that is the required. [Ḥāshiyat al-Subāʿī, p.94]. (3) 'Due to what has passed' meaning that an attribute does not admit another attribute. [Sabāʿī, p.315]
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of divine self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs). It begins with a conditional argument: if God were not self-subsistent, He would either be an accident (ʿaraḍ) or an attribute (ṣifa). Both possibilities are dismissed as impossible for God. The marginal notes expand on the proof: the author presents an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnāʾī). The syllogism proceeds as follows: If God were an attribute, He could not be described with attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), because an attribute cannot receive another attribute. But God is described with such attributes, so He is not an attribute. Therefore, He is a self (dhāt) and must be self-subsistent. The note also references a previous point that an attribute does not admit another attribute.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
عرض
صفة
ذات
قياس استثنائي
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes God is an attribute and then tries to prove He is self-subsistent.
Correction: The proof does not assume God is an attribute; it argues from the impossibility of Him being an attribute to establish that He is a self-subsistent essence.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if God were not self-subsistent, what two possibilities are considered?
A) He would be an accident or an attribute.
B) He would be a body or a substance.
C) He would be a place or time.
D) He would be contingent or impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence is logically necessary because the alternatives of being an accident or an attribute are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s self-subsistence means He depends on nothing, does your life reflect a deep trust in His absolute sufficiency—or do you secretly believe your happiness depends on things He does not need?
?
The proof shows God cannot be an attribute. Do you ever reduce God to a mere concept or attribute in your mind, forgetting that He is a self-subsistent essence who is beyond your categories?
?
You have just seen a logical proof that God must be self-subsistent. How will this truth change the way you pray tonight—not as if you are informing God of your needs, but as one who knows He is completely independent and yet cares for you?

Names Derived from Negative Attributes: al-Jalil, al-Jamil, etc.
Dardeer explains seven divine names derived from negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of seven divine names that derive from negative attributes and how they combine transcendence (tanzih) with positive attributes of perfection.

Core Idea
Divine names like al-Jalil and al-Quddus reflect both transcendence and perfection.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer continues his discussion of negative attributes by explaining several divine names that derive from them. Each name highlights a different aspect of Allah's transcendence, yet also implies positive perfections. As you read, pay attention to how the author ties each name back to the idea of being free from imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation side by side. This will help you get a sense of the flow and the technical terms used.
Notice the poetic line at the beginning that lists the names.
2
Identify the negative attribute basis for each name
For each name, ask: what negative attribute (like being free from defect) is being emphasized? For example, al-Tahir means 'the Pure' – free from all imperfection.
3
Analyze the explanations of al-Jalil and al-Jamil
These two names receive a more detailed treatment. Note how al-Jalil involves both negative and positive attributes, and al-Jamil includes a list of perfections like knowledge and life.
The author adds a spiritual observation about the effects of majesty and beauty on the gnostics.
4
Understand the spiritual effects mentioned
The passage says that due to His majesty, the knowers are humble in awe, and due to His beauty, they are enraptured in love. Reflect on how these names lead to distinct spiritual states.
5
Memorize the seven names and their core meanings
Use the guided breakdown below to memorize each name and its key attribute. This will help you internalize these important divine names.
Consider making flashcards for each name.

القسم الأول: الإلهيات

(والوَلي) أي: مالكُ الخلائقِ ومُتَولّي أمورهم.
(والطاهر) أي: المُنزَّهُ عنْ كُلِّ ما لا يليقُ به.
(القدوس) مِنَ القُدْسِ وهو الطُّهْرُ، أي: العظيم التنْزيهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ .
(والرّبُّ) أي: المالِكُ ومُرَبّي الخلائقِ.
(العلي) أي: المرتفعُ القَدْرِ المُبَرأُ مِنْ كُلِّ عَيْبٍ.
(۱) أي للإرادة مع القدرة.
The first section: Divine Matters. -3- So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamil, and al-Wali, and al-Tahir, al-Quddus, and al-Rabb, al-Ali. Then he branched off from what he mentioned of negative attributes some names and acts of transcendence, and said: (So He) glorified and exalted (al-Jalil) i.e., the great in status, before whose majesty every great one submits, and every formidable thing is deemed small relative to His greatness. And the more apparent is that majesty refers to both negative and positive attributes, not just one of them, as has been said by all. (And al-Jamil) i.e., the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection, of knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and it is only completed by transcendence from every defect and deficiency that is not fitting for the most glorious and protected station. And that includes gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things, for they refer to the will or with power. And due to His majesty, you see the knowers of Him (exalted) humble from His awe, and due to His beauty, you see them enraptured by His love. (And al-Wali) i.e., the owner of creatures and the one who manages their affairs. (And al-Tahir) i.e., the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him. (Al-Quddus) from al-quds which is purity, i.e., the great in transcendence from every deficiency. (And al-Rabb) i.e., the owner and nurturer of creatures. (Al-Ali) i.e., the elevated in rank, the one free from every defect. (1) i.e., to the will with power.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of several divine names that derive from the negative attributes previously discussed. The author begins by stating the section heading 'The first section: Divine Matters' and then quotes the poetic line: 'So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali, al-Tahir, al-Quddus, al-Rabb, al-Ali.' Then the commentary explains each name in order. First, 'al-Jalil' means the great in status; before His majesty every great one submits. The author notes that majesty (jalal) pertains to both negative and positive attributes, not one exclusively. Second, 'al-Jamil' means the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, etc. These attributes are completed by transcendence from any defect. This includes traits like gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon—which ultimately refer to the will or to will combined with power. The author then adds a spiritual observation: due to His majesty, the gnostics are humble in awe; due to His beauty, they are enraptured in love. Third, 'al-Wali' means the owner and manager of all creatures. Fourth, 'al-Tahir' means the one transcendent from everything unbecoming. Fifth, 'al-Quddus' comes from 'quds' (purity), meaning the one greatly transcendent from all deficiency. Sixth, 'al-Rabb' means the owner and nurturer of creatures. Seventh, 'al-Ali' means the elevated in rank, free from all defect. A footnote clarifies that the traits like gentleness refer to the will combined with power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that majesty (jalal) refers only to negative attributes or only to positive attributes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the more apparent view is that majesty refers to both negative and positive attributes together.

Key Takeaway
The seven divine names—al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali, al-Tahir, al-Quddus, al-Rabb, and al-Ali—each highlight a different aspect of Allah's transcendence while also implying positive perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the name al-Jalīl (The Majestic), do you feel genuine awe and humility before Him, or has your heart become hard and unmoved? What one change can you make today to cultivate khashyah (awe) in your worship?
?
The text states that due to His beauty (al-Jamīl), the gnostics are enraptured in love. Have you ever tasted that enrapturement? If not, what competing loves in your heart are blocking you from truly loving Allah for His perfect attributes?
?
Al-Quddūs means 'the One greatly transcendent from every deficiency.' In what areas of your life do you still ascribe deficiency to Allah—perhaps through complaining about His decree or doubting His wisdom? Repent and affirm His absolute perfection right now.

The Majestic and the Beautiful: Al-Jalil & Al-Jamil
Dardeer defines Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil and discusses the scope of majesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names Al-Jalil (the Majestic) and Al-Jamil (the Beautiful) as explained by Imam Dardeer, including the scope of majesty and the effect of these attributes on the knowers.

Core Idea
Allah's majesty and beauty together inspire awe and love in the knower.

In this lesson, we explore two of the most profound divine names: Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil. Imam Dardeer explains what these names signify and how they relate to Allah's attributes, as well as how they affect those who know Him. The passage presents a theological nuance about majesty encompassing both negative and positive attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil
Read the Arabic passage focusing on these two names, along with the translation. Notice how Dardeer defines each name and the additional detail about majesty.
The passage includes a brief description of the effect on knowers at the end.
2
Understand the scope of majesty (al-jalal)
Pay attention to the statement that al-jalal refers to both negative and positive attributes. This is a key theological point—majesty is not limited to one category.
3
Reflect on the effect of these names
Consider how the knowers are described: humbled by majesty and enraptured by beauty. This shows the complementary nature of these attributes.

Source Text

فهو الجليل والجميل والوَلِي * والطاهرُ القُدوسُ والربُّ العَلي
ثُمَّ فَرَّعَ على ما ذكره مِنْ صِفاتِ السُّلوبِ، بعض أسماء وتنزيهاتٍ، فقال: (فهو) سُبحانه وتعالى (الجليل) أي: العظيم الشأن، الذي يخضَعُ لجلاله كُلُّ عظيم، ويُستَحْقَرُ بالنسبة لعظمتِه كُلُّ فَخيم، والأظهرُ أَنَّ الجلال يرجع للصَّفاتِ السلبيّة والكماليّة معًا، لا لإحداهما فقط، كما قيلَ بكُلِّي.
(والجميل) أي: المتَّصِفُ بصفاتِ الجمال والكمال، مِنْ عِلْمٍ وحياةٍ وقُدرة وإرادة وغيرها، وإنما تتمُّ بالتنْزيهِ عنْ كُلِّ عَيب ونقص مما لا يليقُ بالجَنابِ الأعزّ الأحمى، ويندرج في ذلك اللطف والحِلْمُ والكَرَمُ والعَفُوُّ وغَيرُ ذلك مما لا يُحصَى، إِذْ هي ترجِعُ للإرادة أو معَ القُدرةِ.
ولجلاله ترى العارفين به تعالى مِنْ هَيبته خاشعين، ولجماله تراهُم مِنْ حُبِّه موهين.
(والوَلي) أي: مالكُ الخلائقِ ومُتَولّي أمورهم.
(والطاهر) أي: المُنزَّهُ عنْ كُلِّ ما لا يليقُ به.
(القدوس) مِنَ القُدْسِ وهو الطُّهْرُ، أي: العظيم التنْزيهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ .
(والرّبُّ) أي: المالِكُ ومُرَبّي الخلائقِ.
(العلي) أي: المرتفعُ القَدْرِ المُبَرأُ مِنْ كُلِّ عَيْبٍ.
Then He, glorified and exalted, is Al-Jalil (the Majestic), i.e., the great of station, before whose majesty every great one is humbled, and every magnificent thing is considered insignificant in comparison to His greatness. And the more apparent view is that majesty (al-jalal) refers to both the negative and positive attributes together, not to only one of them, as some have said comprehensively. And Al-Jamil (the Beautiful), i.e., the one described with the attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and they are only perfected by transcendence from every defect and imperfection that does not befit the most august and inviolable presence. And included in that are gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things, as they return to will or will together with power. And by His majesty you see those who know Him, exalted, humbled out of awe of Him, and by His beauty you see them enraptured out of love for Him. And Al-Wali (the Patron), i.e., the owner of creatures and the manager of their affairs. And At-Tahir (the Pure), i.e., the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him. And Al-Quddus (the Holy), from al-quds which is purity, i.e., the great in transcendence from every deficiency. And Ar-Rabb (the Lord), i.e., the Owner and the Nurturer of creatures. And Al-Ali (the High), i.e., the elevated in rank, cleared from every defect.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem listing divine names. It first defines Al-Jalil (the Majestic) as the one of great station before whom every great thing is humbled. The author then notes a theological nuance: majesty (al-jalal) encompasses both negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and positive attributes (sifat kamaliyya) together, not just one set. Then Al-Jamil (the Beautiful) is defined as the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection like knowledge, life, power, and will. These are perfected only through transcendence (tanzih) from every defect. The author adds that many attributes like gentleness and forbearance fall under will or will together with power. He then describes the effect of these attributes on the knowers: they are humble before His majesty and enraptured by His beauty. The remaining names are defined concisely: Al-Wali (the Patron) as owner and manager of creatures; At-Tahir (the Pure) as completely transcendent; Al-Quddus (the Holy) as greatly transcendent from deficiency; Ar-Rabb (the Lord) as owner and nurturer; Al-Ali (the High) as elevated in rank and free from defect. The passage follows the order of the poem and explains each name's meaning and theological import.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil together show that Allah's majesty and beauty inspire awe and love, with majesty encompassing all attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Patron, Pure, Holy, Lord, High: Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, Al-Ali
Dardeer defines five divine names emphasizing sovereignty and purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of five divine names that emphasize Allah's sovereignty, purity, and transcendence: Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, and Al-Ali.

Core Idea
Allah is the ultimate Patron, Pure, Holy, Lord, and High—each name underscores His unique majesty.

Having learned about majesty and beauty, we now turn to five more names from the poem. These names highlight Allah's absolute ownership, purity, and elevation above all imperfections. Imam Dardeer provides concise definitions for each, grounding them in the concept of transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the remaining five names
