(والجميل) أي: المتَّصِفُ بصفاتِ الجمال والكمال، مِنْ عِلْمٍ وحياةٍ وقُدرة وإرادة وغيرها، وإنما تتمُّ بالتنْزيهِ عنْ كُلِّ عَيب ونقص مما لا يليقُ بالجَنابِ الأعزّ الأحمى، ويندرج في ذلك اللطف والحِلْمُ والكَرَمُ والعَفُوُّ وغَيرُ ذلك مما لا يُحصَى، إِذْ هي ترجِعُ للإرادة أو معَ القُدرةِ.
ولجلاله ترى العارفين به تعالى مِنْ هَيبته خاشعين، ولجماله تراهُم مِنْ حُبِّه موهين.
(والوَلي) أي: مالكُ الخلائقِ ومُتَولّي أمورهم.
(والطاهر) أي: المُنزَّهُ عنْ كُلِّ ما لا يليقُ به.
(القدوس) مِنَ القُدْسِ وهو الطُّهْرُ، أي: العظيم التنْزيهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ .
(والرّبُّ) أي: المالِكُ ومُرَبّي الخلائقِ.
(العلي) أي: المرتفعُ القَدْرِ المُبَرأُ مِنْ كُلِّ عَيْبٍ.
Then He, glorified and exalted, is Al-Jalil (the Majestic), i.e., the great of station, before whose majesty every great one is humbled, and every magnificent thing is considered insignificant in comparison to His greatness. And the more apparent view is that majesty (al-jalal) refers to both the negative and positive attributes together, not to only one of them, as some have said comprehensively. And Al-Jamil (the Beautiful), i.e., the one described with the attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and they are only perfected by transcendence from every defect and imperfection that does not befit the most august and inviolable presence. And included in that are gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things, as they return to will or will together with power. And by His majesty you see those who know Him, exalted, humbled out of awe of Him, and by His beauty you see them enraptured out of love for Him. And Al-Wali (the Patron), i.e., the owner of creatures and the manager of their affairs. And At-Tahir (the Pure), i.e., the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him. And Al-Quddus (the Holy), from al-quds which is purity, i.e., the great in transcendence from every deficiency. And Ar-Rabb (the Lord), i.e., the Owner and the Nurturer of creatures. And Al-Ali (the High), i.e., the elevated in rank, cleared from every defect.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem listing divine names. It first defines Al-Jalil (the Majestic) as the one of great station before whom every great thing is humbled. The author then notes a theological nuance: majesty (al-jalal) encompasses both negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and positive attributes (sifat kamaliyya) together, not just one set. Then Al-Jamil (the Beautiful) is defined as the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection like knowledge, life, power, and will. These are perfected only through transcendence (tanzih) from every defect. The author adds that many attributes like gentleness and forbearance fall under will or will together with power. He then describes the effect of these attributes on the knowers: they are humble before His majesty and enraptured by His beauty. The remaining names are defined concisely: Al-Wali (the Patron) as owner and manager of creatures; At-Tahir (the Pure) as completely transcendent; Al-Quddus (the Holy) as greatly transcendent from deficiency; Ar-Rabb (the Lord) as owner and nurturer; Al-Ali (the High) as elevated in rank and free from defect. The passage follows the order of the poem and explains each name's meaning and theological import.

Key Terms
الولي
الطاهر
القدوس
الرب
العلي
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of these divine names means 'the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him'?
A) Al-Wali
B) At-Tahir
C) Al-Quddus
D) Ar-Rabb

Key Takeaway
These five names—Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, Al-Ali—collectively affirm Allah's absolute ownership, purity, and transcendence over all creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Wali owns and manages all your affairs. What specific worry are you still clutching that you have not surrendered to His management? Will you verbally declare your trust in Him right now?
?
If Allah is At-Tahir, completely pure and transcendent from all that does not befit Him, what impurity in your life—a habit, a thought, a relationship—have you tolerated that contradicts His purity? What one concrete action will you take today to seek His purification?
?
Al-Quddus is the great in transcendence from every deficiency. When you fail in worship or fall into sin, you are acting as if deficiency is acceptable. How can you use the knowledge of His holiness to repent sincerely and raise your standard of devotion starting today?

Allah is Exalted Above Foolishness (Safah)
Dardeer defines safah and proves its impossibility for Allah from perfect creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of safah (foolishness) and why it is impossible for Allah based on His attributes of perfect management, awareness, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Safah is placing things in the wrong place; Allah’s perfect wisdom excludes all foolishness.

This lesson examines another attribute of Allah’s perfection: His transcendence above safah (foolishness). We will define this term, see how it is negated from Allah by citing His attributes, and reflect on a profound observation from the gnostics about the perfection of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from definition to proof to testimony.
The passage is concise but rich in meaning.
2
Define safah and note its negation
Identify the definition of safah as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place.' Understand that Allah is above this because He is al-Mudīr, al-Khabīr, al-ʿAlīm.
Safah is not just ignorance; it is misplacement.
3
Connect the negation to Allah's attributes
Think about how the attributes of Manager, All-Aware, All-Knowing logically preclude any foolish action. Perfect knowledge and awareness ensure perfect placement.
This is a rational proof from attributes.
4
Reflect on the gnostic's statement
Consider the statement: 'There is not in possibility anything more wonderful than what has come to be.' Discuss what it reveals about the perfection of creation and why it supports the negation of safah.
This is an illustrative evidence from observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكيم. (و) منزهة أيضًا عن (السفه) وهو: وضع الشيء في غير محله، إذ هو المدير الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعض أهل العرفان لما شاهد من عجيب الإتقان: ليس لي الإمكان أبدع مما كان.
Al-Ḥakīm (The Wise). And He is also transcendent above foolishness (safah), which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, for He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Hence some of the people of spiritual insight, when they witnessed the wonder of perfect order, said: 'There is not in possibility anything more wonderful than what has come to be.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of Allah's transcendence above safah (foolishness). The author first defines safah as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place.' He then negates this from Allah by citing His attributes: He is al-Mudīr (the Manager), al-Khabīr (the All-Aware), and al-ʿAlīm (the All-Knowing). Since He perfectly manages and knows all things, He never acts foolishly. As evidence, the author quotes a statement from 'some of the people of spiritual insight' (understood to be Imam al-Ghazali) who, upon observing the exquisite perfection of creation, declared that nothing in the realm of possibility could be more excellent than what Allah has actually brought into existence. This statement underscores that Allah's creation is optimal, leaving no room for foolishness or imperfection. The passage thus combines a clear definition, a rational proof from Allah's attributes, and an illustrative testimony from the gnostics.

Key Terms
السفه
وضع الشيء في غير محله
المدير
الخبير
العليم
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the gnostic's statement implies that Allah could not create a better world.
Correction: The statement actually affirms that what Allah created is perfectly suited to His wisdom; it does not limit His power but highlights the perfection of His actual creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of safah?
A) Ignorance of future events.
B) Placing a thing in other than its proper place.
C) Lying and deception.
D) Inability to create.

Key Takeaway
Allah's perfect wisdom and knowledge preclude any foolishness in His actions; everything He does is perfectly placed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the areas of your life that feel chaotic or misplaced, do you truly believe that Allah, al-Mudīr, has arranged them perfectly? What specific thought patterns lead you to doubt His wisdom, and how can you actively replace them with trust?
?
Consider one decision you made recently that you now regret—did you act without full awareness or knowledge? How does knowing that Allah never misplaces anything challenge you to seek His guidance before you act?

Al-Ghazali's Statement and Its Interpretations
Context and four interpretive responses to al-Ghazali's saying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and context of al-Ghazali's controversial saying and the four main responses that preserve divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's statement is about God's wisdom, not incapability.

Imam al-Ghazali's saying 'There is not in possibility anything more beautiful than what has been' has puzzled scholars for centuries. This lesson unpacks the statement and the four key interpretations that show it does not diminish God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the context on divine purity
Notice how the author links the statement to God's transcendence above folly (safah).
2
Learn the statement itself
Read the Arabic saying and its translation carefully.
3
Analyze the four responses
Each response explains the statement in a way that avoids implying limit on divine power.
4
Understand the two sides of possibility
Abu Talib al-Makki and al-Ghazali assume possibility on different sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) مُنرَّةٌ أيضًا عن (السَّفَه) وهو: وضْعُ الشَّيء في غَيرِ تَحَلَّه، إِذْ هو المدير الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعضُ أهْلِ العِرفانِ لَما شَاهَدَ مِنْ عجِيبِ الإتقان: ليس لي الإمكان أبدَعُ مما كان (٢). (2) هذا هو قول حجة الإسلام الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه ، وقد استشكل أهل العلم هذا القول لإيهامه العجز وهو عليه تعالى محال، وأجابوا بأجوبة منها: 1 . أن المراد إمكان الخلائق، فالمعنى ليس في إمكان الخلائق تغيير ما أراده الله وأبدعه. .٢ ومنها أن المراد إمكانه تعالى باعتبار تعلق علمه أزلا بإيجاد هذا العالم على هذا النظام، وتعلق القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على وفق ما خصصته الإرادة وسبق به العلم، فالمعنى ليس في الإمكان شيء يقبل الزيادة أو النقص على خلاف ما سبق في العلم. .٣ قال الشيخ الأمير : هذا كلام مشوش عليه أو سرى له من الفلاسفة. ٤. أو أنه محمول على ما تسعه عقولنا كما قال أبو طالب المكي في كتاب التوكل: اعلم يقينا أن الله لو جعل الخلائق كلهم على علم أعلمهم به وعقل أعقلهم وحكمة أحكمهم .. ثم قيل لهم دبروا الملك بما أعطيتكم من العلوم والعقول لما زاد تدبيرهم على ما تراه من تدبيره تعالى جناح بعوضة ٩٦ .اهـ. فعدم الإمكان افترضه أبو طالب في جانب الخلائق والغزالي في جانب الحق سبحانه. وقد شنع على الإمام الغزالي جماعة قالوا: إن هذا فيه نسبة العجز لقدرة الإله، وذكر الشعراني في اليواقيت عن ابن عربي انه كلام في غاية التحقيق، لأنه ما ثم إلا رتبتان قدم وحدوث، فالمخلوق له رتبة الحدوث، فلو خلق الحق تعالى ما خلق ما خرج عن هذه الرتبة فلا يقال: هل يقدر الحق أن يخلق قديما مثله فإنه سؤال مهمل ،لاستحالته ولما سأل صاحب الإبريز شيخه عبد العزيز الدباغ عن مقالة الغزالي لم يصرح له بالإنكار ولا بالتأويل وإنما قال له: «القدرة لا تحصر والرب لا يعجزه شيء، هذا وقد اختلف العلماء في مقالة الغزالي على ثلاثة أقوال: طائفة ردتها، وطائفة أولتها، وطائفة كذبت نسبتها إليه كما سبق الإشارة إلى ذلك وصاحب الإبريز رد جميع التأويلات وقال في آخر كلامه قد حماني الله تعالى من أبي حامد بشيخنا، وذلك أني لما عزمت على رد هذه المسألة وقف الشيخ علي منامًا فملأ قلبي بتعظيم أبي حامد حتى صارت ردودي تتوجه إلى المسألة ولم ينله منها شيء ولم يجر على لساني إلا تعظيمه». اهـ. وأرى أن الحق مع الطائفة التي أولتها لا مع التي كذبتها ولا مع التي ردتها .
And (He is) also purified from (folly) which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, since He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Therefore, some of the people of spiritual knowledge said, when they witnessed the wondrous precision: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been' (2). (2) This is the saying of the Proof of Islam, Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him. The scholars have found this saying problematic because it suggests incapability, which is impossible for Him, Exalted is He. They answered with several answers, including: 1. That what is meant is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is that it is not within the possibility of creatures to change what Allah has willed and created. 2. Another is that what is meant is His possibility, Exalted is He, considering the attachment of His knowledge from eternity to bringing this world into existence in this system, and the actual attachment of power is only in accordance with what the will has specified and what knowledge has preceded, so the meaning is that there is nothing in possibility that admits increase or decrease contrary to what has preceded in knowledge. 3. Al-Shaykh al-Amir said: This is confused speech or it came to him from the philosophers. 4. Or that it is interpreted according to what our intellects can encompass, as Abu Talib al-Makki said in the book al-Tawakkul: 'Know with certainty that if Allah were to make all creatures possess the knowledge with which He knows them, the intellect with which they are most intelligent, and the wisdom with which they are most wise... then they were told to manage the kingdom with what I have given you of sciences and intellects, their management would not add to what you see of His management, Exalted is He, even a gnat's wing.' 96. So the lack of possibility was assumed by Abu Talib on the side of creatures, and by al-Ghazali on the side of the Truth, Exalted is He. Some groups have attacked Imam al-Ghazali, saying that this attributes incapability to the power of Allah. Al-Sharani mentioned in al-Yawaqit from Ibn 'Arabi that it is speech of extreme verification, because there are only two ranks: preeternity and origination. The creature has the rank of origination, so if the Truth, Exalted is He, had created what He created, it would not depart from this rank, so one does not say: 'Can the Truth create something preeternal like Him?' because it is a meaningless question, due to its impossibility. When the author of al-Ibriz asked his shaykh 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dabbagh about al-Ghazali's statement, he did not explicitly reject it nor interpret it, but rather said to him: 'Power is not limited, and the Lord is not incapacitated by anything. Indeed, the scholars have differed concerning al-Ghazali's statement in three opinions: a group rejected it, a group interpreted it, and a group declared its attribution to him to be false, as has been indicated previously. The author of al-Ibriz rejected all interpretations and said at the end of his speech: 'Allah has protected me from Abu Hamid through our shaykh, for when I resolved to refute this issue, the shaykh appeared to me in a dream and filled my heart with reverence for Abu Hamid, such that my refutations were directed to the issue itself and nothing of it touched him, nor did my tongue utter anything but veneration of him.' End. I see that the truth is with the group that interpreted it, not with those who declared it false nor with those who rejected it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial statement attributed to Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been.' The author first places this in the context of discussing divine transcendence above folly (safah). Al-Ghazali's statement seemed to imply that God could not create a more perfect world, which suggests incapability. The text then presents several responses: (1) The statement refers to the possibility of creatures, meaning they cannot alter God's creation. (2) It refers to God's own possibility in light of His eternal knowledge and will—since God willed this specific world, no other world could have been created. (3) Some dismiss it as confused speech influenced by philosophy. (4) Others restrict it to what our minds can comprehend, citing Abu Talib al-Makki's idea that even the wisest creatures cannot improve upon God's creation. The passage then notes the three scholarly positions: rejection, interpretation, or denial of authenticity. The author (the commentator, likely al-Sawi) sides with those who interpret it. The story of al-Dabbagh's dream shows a reverence for al-Ghazali that prevents direct criticism. The discussion illustrates the complexity of theological discourse and the importance of preserving the integrity of great scholars while addressing problematic statements.

Key Terms
ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان
السفه
إمكان
القدرة
الغزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the FIRST response interpreting al-Ghazali's statement?
A) It refers to God's own possibility
B) It is confused speech from philosophers
C) It means creatures cannot change God's creation
D) It is about our limited intellects

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's controversial statement can be understood in multiple ways that all affirm Allah's absolute wisdom and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a difficult situation in your life that you wish were different, do you truly believe that there is nothing more perfect than what God has decreed? Or does your heart secretly wish for a different outcome, doubting the perfection of His plan? Examine your innermost reaction and ask yourself: are you content with His decree as the best possible?
?
Al-Ghazali's statement was misunderstood as suggesting God is incapable. Have you ever, in your own life, thought that God's command or decree was lacking wisdom or power? Where specifically? What would it take for you to surrender that doubt and affirm, as the scholars did, that His knowledge and power are perfect?
?
The scholars provided interpretations to protect the understanding of God's attributes. In your own learning, have you ever clung to a literal reading of a text or a scholar's saying without seeking its proper interpretation? Are you willing to revisit your beliefs and ensure they align with the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah? Take a moment to identify one area where you need to re-evaluate your understanding.

Scholarly Opinions on al-Ghazali's Statement
Three scholarly opinions and author's conclusion on al-Ghazali's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on al-Ghazali's saying and why the author sides with the interpreters.

Core Idea
The disagreement shows how scholars protect both divine power and scholarly legacy.

After presenting the four responses, the passage turns to the broader scholarly debate. Some rejected al-Ghazali's statement outright, others reinterpreted it, and some denied he ever said it. This lesson explores these positions and the author's resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the criticism and Ibn 'Arabi's defense
Notice how Ibn 'Arabi argues the statement is actually a profound truth.
2
Learn the three scholarly opinions
Identify the three groups: rejecters, interpreters, and those who deny attribution.
3
Consider the dream story
The author of al-Ibriz's dream shows reverence for al-Ghazali while still critiquing the statement.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
The author explicitly sides with the interpreters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) مُنرَّةٌ أيضًا عن (السَّفَه) وهو: وضْعُ الشَّيء في غَيرِ تَحَلَّه، إِذْ هو المدير الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعضُ أهْلِ العِرفانِ لَما شَاهَدَ مِنْ عجِيبِ الإتقان: ليس لي الإمكان أبدَعُ مما كان (٢). (2) هذا هو قول حجة الإسلام الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه ، وقد استشكل أهل العلم هذا القول لإيهامه العجز وهو عليه تعالى محال، وأجابوا بأجوبة منها: 1 . أن المراد إمكان الخلائق، فالمعنى ليس في إمكان الخلائق تغيير ما أراده الله وأبدعه. .٢ ومنها أن المراد إمكانه تعالى باعتبار تعلق علمه أزلا بإيجاد هذا العالم على هذا النظام، وتعلق القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على وفق ما خصصته الإرادة وسبق به العلم، فالمعنى ليس في الإمكان شيء يقبل الزيادة أو النقص على خلاف ما سبق في العلم. .٣ قال الشيخ الأمير : هذا كلام مشوش عليه أو سرى له من الفلاسفة. ٤. أو أنه محمول على ما تسعه عقولنا كما قال أبو طالب المكي في كتاب التوكل: اعلم يقينا أن الله لو جعل الخلائق كلهم على علم أعلمهم به وعقل أعقلهم وحكمة أحكمهم .. ثم قيل لهم دبروا الملك بما أعطيتكم من العلوم والعقول لما زاد تدبيرهم على ما تراه من تدبيره تعالى جناح بعوضة ٩٦ .اهـ. فعدم الإمكان افترضه أبو طالب في جانب الخلائق والغزالي في جانب الحق سبحانه. وقد شنع على الإمام الغزالي جماعة قالوا: إن هذا فيه نسبة العجز لقدرة الإله، وذكر الشعراني في اليواقيت عن ابن عربي انه كلام في غاية التحقيق، لأنه ما ثم إلا رتبتان قدم وحدوث، فالمخلوق له رتبة الحدوث، فلو خلق الحق تعالى ما خلق ما خرج عن هذه الرتبة فلا يقال: هل يقدر الحق أن يخلق قديما مثله فإنه سؤال مهمل ،لاستحالته ولما سأل صاحب الإبريز شيخه عبد العزيز الدباغ عن مقالة الغزالي لم يصرح له بالإنكار ولا بالتأويل وإنما قال له: «القدرة لا تحصر والرب لا يعجزه شيء، هذا وقد اختلف العلماء في مقالة الغزالي على ثلاثة أقوال: طائفة ردتها، وطائفة أولتها، وطائفة كذبت نسبتها إليه كما سبق الإشارة إلى ذلك وصاحب الإبريز رد جميع التأويلات وقال في آخر كلامه قد حماني الله تعالى من أبي حامد بشيخنا، وذلك أني لما عزمت على رد هذه المسألة وقف الشيخ علي منامًا فملأ قلبي بتعظيم أبي حامد حتى صارت ردودي تتوجه إلى المسألة ولم ينله منها شيء ولم يجر على لساني إلا تعظيمه». اهـ. وأرى أن الحق مع الطائفة التي أولتها لا مع التي كذبتها ولا مع التي ردتها .
And (He is) also purified from (folly) which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, since He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Therefore, some of the people of spiritual knowledge said, when they witnessed the wondrous precision: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been' (2). (2) This is the saying of the Proof of Islam, Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him. The scholars have found this saying problematic because it suggests incapability, which is impossible for Him, Exalted is He. They answered with several answers, including: 1. That what is meant is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is that it is not within the possibility of creatures to change what Allah has willed and created. 2. Another is that what is meant is His possibility, Exalted is He, considering the attachment of His knowledge from eternity to bringing this world into existence in this system, and the actual attachment of power is only in accordance with what the will has specified and what knowledge has preceded, so the meaning is that there is nothing in possibility that admits increase or decrease contrary to what has preceded in knowledge. 3. Al-Shaykh al-Amir said: This is confused speech or it came to him from the philosophers. 4. Or that it is interpreted according to what our intellects can encompass, as Abu Talib al-Makki said in the book al-Tawakkul: 'Know with certainty that if Allah were to make all creatures possess the knowledge with which He knows them, the intellect with which they are most intelligent, and the wisdom with which they are most wise... then they were told to manage the kingdom with what I have given you of sciences and intellects, their management would not add to what you see of His management, Exalted is He, even a gnat's wing.' 96. So the lack of possibility was assumed by Abu Talib on the side of creatures, and by al-Ghazali on the side of the Truth, Exalted is He. Some groups have attacked Imam al-Ghazali, saying that this attributes incapability to the power of Allah. Al-Sharani mentioned in al-Yawaqit from Ibn 'Arabi that it is speech of extreme verification, because there are only two ranks: preeternity and origination. The creature has the rank of origination, so if the Truth, Exalted is He, had created what He created, it would not depart from this rank, so one does not say: 'Can the Truth create something preeternal like Him?' because it is a meaningless question, due to its impossibility. When the author of al-Ibriz asked his shaykh 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dabbagh about al-Ghazali's statement, he did not explicitly reject it nor interpret it, but rather said to him: 'Power is not limited, and the Lord is not incapacitated by anything. Indeed, the scholars have differed concerning al-Ghazali's statement in three opinions: a group rejected it, a group interpreted it, and a group declared its attribution to him to be false, as has been indicated previously. The author of al-Ibriz rejected all interpretations and said at the end of his speech: 'Allah has protected me from Abu Hamid through our shaykh, for when I resolved to refute this issue, the shaykh appeared to me in a dream and filled my heart with reverence for Abu Hamid, such that my refutations were directed to the issue itself and nothing of it touched him, nor did my tongue utter anything but veneration of him.' End. I see that the truth is with the group that interpreted it, not with those who declared it false nor with those who rejected it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial statement attributed to Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been.' The author first places this in the context of discussing divine transcendence above folly (safah). Al-Ghazali's statement seemed to imply that God could not create a more perfect world, which suggests incapability. The text then presents several responses: (1) The statement refers to the possibility of creatures, meaning they cannot alter God's creation. (2) It refers to God's own possibility in light of His eternal knowledge and will—since God willed this specific world, no other world could have been created. (3) Some dismiss it as confused speech influenced by philosophy. (4) Others restrict it to what our minds can comprehend, citing Abu Talib al-Makki's idea that even the wisest creatures cannot improve upon God's creation. The passage then notes the three scholarly positions: rejection, interpretation, or denial of authenticity. The author (the commentator, likely al-Sawi) sides with those who interpret it. The story of al-Dabbagh's dream shows a reverence for al-Ghazali that prevents direct criticism. The discussion illustrates the complexity of theological discourse and the importance of preserving the integrity of great scholars while addressing problematic statements.

ابن عربي
صاحب الإبريز
السفه
إمكان

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The three scholarly opinions on al-Ghazali's statement show how scholars protect both divine perfection and the integrity of great scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a statement from a respected scholar that troubles you, which of the three groups do you instinctively join? Does your reaction protect divine perfection or risk attacking the scholar's legacy? What would it take for you to direct your refutation at the issue rather than the person, as al-Ibriz learned?
?
Have you ever felt justified in dismissing a scholar's words to feel 'safe' in your faith? This lesson shows that interpretation is the truth. Are you willing to labor over a difficult saying instead of rejecting it? What personal attachment to being 'correct' might you need to surrender?
?
The author of al-Ibriz had a dream that filled his heart with reverence for al-Ghazali. Have you ever caught yourself disrespecting a scholar of the past in your heart? How can you train yourself to separate critique of an idea from attack on the person, especially when the idea seems problematic?

Divine Attributes

6 min
Classification of Divine Attributes into Existence, Negative, and Psychic
Dardeer classifies divine attributes into existence, negative, and psychic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of divine attributes in Ash'ari theology and the logical ordering of purification before adornment.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are classified by existence, absence, and relation to essence.

Now that the negative attributes have been established, Dardeer turns to the positive attributes of God. He introduces the famous distinction between attributes of meanings, negative attributes, and psychic attributes. This classification is essential to understanding the Ash'ari framework for talking about God's attributes without compromising His uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the order of presentation
Notice that the author places the discussion of negative attributes first, then attributes of meanings. He explains this order as purification (takhliya) before adornment (tahliya).
This reflects the principle that removing imperfections precedes affirming perfections.
2
Define attributes of meanings
Attributes of meanings are existence attributes (wujudiyya)—those that have real existence in themselves, whether eternal or created. Examples include God's knowledge and power, as well as human knowledge and power.
3
Distinguish psychic from meaningful attributes
Psychic attributes (nafsiyya) are necessary for the essence without a cause (e.g., existence for God). Meaningful attributes (ma'nawiyya) are caused by a cause and are relative to the meanings (e.g., being knowing due to knowledge).
This division refines the category of non-existence, non-negative attributes.
4
Apply the classification to created examples
The author includes examples from creation (our knowledge, power, whiteness) to show that 'attributes of meanings' is a broad term applicable to any existence attribute, not exclusively divine.
This prevents the misconception that the term only applies to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات
ولما فرغ مِنَ الكلام على الصفات السلبيّةِ شَرَعَ في بيان صفاتِ المعاني، وقدَّمها لأنها من باب التخلية، والمعاني مِنْ بابِ التحلية، وشأن التخليةِ أنْ تَقَدَّمَ على التحلية
فقال:
ثُمَّ المعاني سبعةُ للرائي * أيْ: عِلْمُهُ المحيط بالأشياء
(ثُمَّ المعاني) أَيْ: ثُمَّ بِعْدَ أَنْ عَرَفتَ ما تقدَّمَ مِنَ النَّفْسِيِّةِ والسلبية، فيجِبُ عليك معرفةُ الصِّفاتِ المُسماة بالمعاني؛ لأنَّ كُلَّ واحِدةٍ منها معنى قائم بذاتِه تعالى.
بيان
ومُرادهم بصفاتِ المعاني الصِّفاتُ الوُجوديّةُ، أي: التي لها وُجودٌ في نفسها، صفات قديمةً كانتْ أو حادثةً، كعلمه وقدرته تعالى، وكعِلْمِنا وقدرتنا، والبياض والسَّوادِ.
والحاصل : أَنَّ الصفات إنْ كانتْ وُجوديّةٌ سُمّيتْ صِفاتِ معان .. وإن لم تكن وجوديّةً ، فإنْ كان مدلولها عدَمَ أمر لا يليقُ سُمّيت سلبية، وإن لم يكُنْ مدلولها عدَما، فإن كانتْ واجبةً للذاتِ - ما دامتِ الذاتُ غَيرَ - سُمّيت صفةً نفسيّةً وحالا نفْسيّةً، كالوُجودِ وكالتحيز للجرْمِ وقبوله للأغراض . وإن كانت معللةً بعلة بأن كانتْ واجبةً للذات ما دامت علتُها سُمّيت معنوية كالعالمية والقادرية، أي: كون الذات المنصفةِ بالعِلْمِ عالمة، وكَونِ الذات النوارية بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي (سبعة للرائي) أي: الناظر المتأمل.
The First Section: Divine Matters. And when he finished speaking on the negative attributes, he began explaining the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), and he presented them because they are in the chapter of purification, and the meanings are in the chapter of adornment, and the way of purification is that it precedes adornment. He said: Then the meanings are seven for the observer: that is, His knowledge encompassing things. (Then the meanings) i.e., then after you have known what preceded of the psychic (al-nafsiyya) and negative (al-salbiyya), it is obligatory upon you to know the attributes called meanings (al-maʿānī), because each one of them is a meaning subsisting by His Essence, exalted is He. Explanation: And their intent by 'attributes of meanings' is the existence attributes (al-ṣifāt al-wujūdiyya), i.e., those that have existence in themselves, attributes whether eternal or originated, like His knowledge and power, and like our knowledge and power, and whiteness and blackness. And the conclusion is: that attributes, if they are existence attributes, they are called attributes of meanings. And if they are not existence attributes, then if their indication is the absence of an unfitting matter, they are called negative. And if their indication is not absence, then if they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence is other, they are called a psychic attribute and a psychic state, like existence and like the body's occupying space and its receiving accidents. And if they are caused by a cause, in that they are necessary for the essence as long as their cause exists, they are called meaningful (maʿnawiyya), like being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) and being powerful (al-qādariyya), i.e., the essence described with knowledge being knowing, and the essence described with power being powerful, in relation to the meanings, and they are (seven for the observer), i.e., the contemplative observer.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes. The author begins by stating that after finishing the discussion of negative attributes, he now turns to the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). He orders them this way because the removal of imperfections (takhliya) comes before adornment (taḥliya). Then he quotes the poetic line: 'Then the meanings are seven for the observer: that is, His knowledge encompassing things.' He explains that after understanding the psychic and negative attributes, one must know the attributes called meanings (maʿānī), because each is a meaning subsisting in God's essence. He then gives a 'bayān' (clarification): by attributes of meanings they mean existence attributes (wujūdiyya), i.e., attributes that have existence in themselves, whether eternal or created, citing examples like God's knowledge and power, and also our knowledge and power, and whiteness and blackness. This shows that the term 'attribute of meaning' applies broadly to any existence attribute. He then gives the 'conclusion' (al-ḥāṣil): attributes are divided into three categories. First, if they are existence attributes (wujūdiyya), they are called attributes of meanings (ṣifāt maʿānī). Second, if they are not existence attributes, and their indication is the absence of something unfitting, they are called negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya). Third, if they are not existence attributes and their indication is not absence, then: (a) if they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists (without a cause), they are called psychic attributes (ṣifa nafsiyya) or psychic states, like existence for God or like a body's occupying space and receiving accidents; (b) if they are caused by a cause (muʿallala bi ʿilla), in that they are necessary for the essence as long as the cause exists, they are called meaningful attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya), like being knowing (derived from knowledge) and being powerful (derived from power). These meaningful attributes are relative to the meanings (the existence attributes). Finally, he reiterates that the meanings (attributes of meanings) are seven for the contemplative observer. This classification is foundational for understanding the Ashʿarī framework of attributes.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات السلبية
الصفات النفسية
الصفات المعنوية
التخلية
التحلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes of meanings only apply to God and not to creatures.
Correction: The author explicitly includes created examples (our knowledge, power, whiteness) to show that 'attributes of meanings' refers to any existence attribute, whether divine or created.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes a psychic attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) from a meaningful attribute (ṣifa maʿnawiyya)?
A) Psychic attributes are existence attributes, while meaningful attributes are not.
B) Psychic attributes are necessary for the essence without a cause, while meaningful attributes are caused by a cause.
C) Psychic attributes are negative, while meaningful attributes are positive.
D) Psychic attributes are only for God, while meaningful attributes are for creatures.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari classification of divine attributes uses existence, negation, and psychic (necessary/caused) to systematically describe God's perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach God's attributes as mere intellectual categories, or do you let them transform how you live? For example, knowing that God's knowledge is an existence attribute, do you live with the awareness that He sees everything? What is one action you will change this week to reflect that awareness?
?
The author says purification (removing negatives) comes before adornment (acquiring positives). What is one negative trait—like arrogance or laziness—that you need to remove before you can truly embody a positive attribute like knowledge? Write down one concrete step you will take today to begin that purification.
?
If you truly believed that every existence attribute of God has an effect on your life—His power enables you, His knowledge guides you—would you rely on Him more? What is one area of your life where you are still depending on yourself rather than on His attributes? Commit to turning that area over to Him this week.

Defining Divine Knowledge: Eternal, All-Encompassing, Infallible
Dardeer defines the attribute of divine knowledge as eternal and all-encompassing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine knowledge as an eternal attribute that discloses all things (existents and non-existents) with certainty, without any possibility of error.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is eternal, covers everything, and never errs.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer unpacks the definition of divine knowledge. He clarifies that God's knowledge is not limited to what exists; it encompasses even non-existent possibilities and impossibilities. This establishes the absolute comprehensiveness and infallibility of divine knowledge, distinguishing it from human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's meaning.
Notice the precision in the wording.
2
Note the scope of 'things'
The author clarifies that 'things' includes all three categories: necessary, possible, and impossible. This is broader than the common usage.
3
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence that defines divine knowledge: an eternal attribute that discloses existents and non-existents as they truly are.
Pay attention to 'disclosure that does not admit the opposite'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why including non-existents and the impossibility of error matters for understanding God's knowledge.
This distinguishes divine knowledge from human opinion and probability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ فَسَّرَها بقوله: (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عُطِفَ عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كُلها. واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليسَ مُراده بالأَشْياءِ الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارفُ عِندَهُم. وهو: صفةٌ أزليّةً تنكشِفُ بها الموجودات والمعدوماتُ على ما هي عليه انكشافا لا يحتَمِلُ النقيض بوَجهِ
Then he interpreted it by his saying: (that is, His knowledge) and what is conjoined to it (encompassing things) all of them: their necessary, possible, and impossible. So his intent by 'things' is not only existents as is customary among them. And it is: an eternal attribute by which existents and non-existents are disclosed as they are, a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine knowledge. The author is commenting on the phrase 'that is, His knowledge encompassing things' from the poetic line. He explains that 'things' here includes all things: the necessary (wājib), the possible (jāʾiz), and the impossible (mustaḥīl). He clarifies that 'things' is not limited to existents (mawjūdāt) as is common in ordinary usage. Then he gives a precise definition: divine knowledge is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) by which existents and non-existents are disclosed as they truly are, with a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way. This means it is infallible and certain, unlike opinion (ẓann) or assumption (iʿtiqād). The inclusion of non-existents (maʿdūmāt) emphasizes that God's knowledge covers everything, even what does not exist, in its true state. The term 'disclosure' (inkishāf) is carefully chosen but later commentators note it might imply change; however, here it is understood as timeless manifestation.

Key Terms
العلم
صفة أزلية
الموجودات
المعدومات
انكشاف
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge only pertains to existing things.
Correction: The passage explicitly includes non-existents (maʿdūmāt) and the categories of impossible and possible, showing that God's knowledge encompasses everything in its true state, whether existent or not.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Knowledge that is infallible and eternal means Allah can never be surprised by your choices. Since He already knows every moment of your life, are you living with the awareness that nothing is hidden? Commit to one area where you will start living more openly before Him.

The Definition of Allah's Knowledge
Definition and explanation of eternal divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical definition of Allah's knowledge as an eternal, comprehensive attribute that discloses all things as they are, without change or temporal limitation.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is eternal, comprehensive, and unchanged by time.

Before exploring the divine attribute of knowledge, we need a precise definition that guards against human limitations. The author presents knowledge as an eternal attribute that encompasses not only existents but also possibilities and impossibilities. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting how the author defines knowledge as an eternal attribute that discloses all things.
Pay attention to the phrase 'does not admit its opposite' — it distinguishes knowledge from opinion.
2
Understand the scope of 'encompassing all things'
The passage clarifies that 'things' includes necessary, possible, and impossible, not just existents. This shows the vastness of divine knowledge.
3
Compare with created knowledge
Notice the contrasts: divine knowledge is not preceded by ignorance, does not change with time, and is not acquired.
This highlights the transcendence of Allah's knowledge.
4
Examine the ta'wīl of the verse
The author interprets Q. 2:143 to resolve a potential misunderstanding. Understand why the interpretation shifts the lām to consequence rather than causation.
This is a key example of how kalam handles scriptural expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ فَسَّرَها بقوله: (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عُطِفَ عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كُلها. واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليسَ مُراده بالأَشْياءِ الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارفُ عِندَهُم. وهو: صفةٌ أزليّةً تنكشِفُ بها الموجودات والمعدوماتُ على ما هي عليه انكشافا لا يحتَمِلُ النقيض بوَجه. (۱) علمه تعالى محيط إحاطة تامة بكل شيء وليس كالعلم الحادث لأنه لم يسبق بجهل ولم يتجدد عند تجدد الأشياء فقد كانت معلومة له تعالى قبل وجودها على ما ستكون عليه، وكون الأشياء ماضية أو حاضرة أو مستقبلة لا يوجب ذلك تغيرًا في العلم وإنما هي أطوار في المعلومات، وعلمه تعالى ليس كسبًا، وما ورد مما يوهم خلاف ذلك فمؤول كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلَنَا القِبْلَةَ التِي كُنتَ عَلَيْهَا إِلا لِنَعْلَمَ ... الآية [البقرة : ١٤٣]، فإن ذلك مؤول بجعل اللام للعاقبة لا للتعليل، والمعنى: فعلنا ذلك فترتب عليه كذا دون أن يكون باعثًا على الفعل إلا أنه مرتب عليه. (۲) المراد بالانكشاف التمييز والاتضاح إن قلت التعبير بـ ينكشف يوهم حدوث الانكشاف لأن المضارع يدل على الحال والاستقبال، وهو لا يناسب علم الله تعالى! أجيب بأن الأفعال الواقعة في التعاريف مجردة عن الزمان، ولا دلالة لها عليه فكأنه قيل : صفة يحصل بها انكشاف ما تعلقت به، كذا قيل، وأنت خبير بأن الفعل وإن كان الملاحظ منه المصدر وهو الانكشاف إلا أن التعبير بالانكشاف هنا غير لائق من جهة أنه انفعال يوهم حدوث إيضاح بعد خفاء. [حاشية الصاوي، ص ٤٤] (۳) دخل فيه العلم نفسه، فيعلم بعلمه علمه، كما يعلم به ذاته وسائر صفاته، لأن كل صفة ليست من صفات التأثير لا يستحيل تعلقها بنفسها وبغيرها. [حاشية الصاوي، ص ٤٤] (٤) أي على الوجه الذي هي عليه . [حاشية السباعي، ص ٩٩] (٥) مخرج للظن والاعتقاد والوهم. [ حاشية السباعي، ص ٩٩]
Then he explained it by his saying: 'i.e., His knowledge' and what is conjoined to it 'encompassing all things' — necessary, possible, and impossible thereof. So he does not mean by 'things' only existents as is customary among them. And it (knowledge) is an eternal attribute by which existents and non-existents are disclosed (tankashif) as they are, a disclosure that does not admit of its opposite in any way. (1) His knowledge, exalted be He, encompasses everything completely and is not like originated knowledge because it was not preceded by ignorance and does not change when things change; rather, things were known to Him before their existence as they will be, and whether they are past, present, or future does not cause change in knowledge but are only states in the known things. His knowledge is not acquired, and what suggests otherwise is interpreted, such as His saying 'only that We might know' (Quran 2:143), which is interpreted by making the lām for consequence, not for causation, meaning: We did that so this resulted, without being a motive for the act. (2) What is meant by disclosure (inkishāf) is discrimination and clarity. If you say: the expression 'inkishāf' suggests the occurrence of disclosure because the imperfect verb indicates present and future, which does not suit Allah's knowledge, the answer is that verbs used in definitions are abstracted from time and have no temporal indication; it is as if one said: an attribute through which disclosure of what it is related to obtains. But you are aware that though the verbal noun (maṣdar) — which is disclosure — is intended, the expression 'inkishāf' is inappropriate because it is a passive state implying a fresh manifestation after concealment. (3) It includes knowledge itself, so He knows His knowledge with His knowledge, just as He knows His essence and all His attributes, because any attribute that is not an attribute of effecting (ta'thīr) is not impossible to be related to itself and to others. (4) i.e., in the manner that it is. (5) It excludes conjecture (ẓann), belief (iʿtiqād), and illusion (wahm).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of knowledge (al-ʿilm). It begins by explaining the author's phrase 'His knowledge encompassing all things' — this includes necessary, possible, and impossible things (wājib, jāʾiz, mustaḥīl), not just existents. The formal definition is given: 'an eternal attribute by which existents and non-existents are disclosed as they are, a disclosure that does not admit its opposite in any way.' The footnotes then elaborate. First, Allah's knowledge is fully comprehensive and unlike created knowledge: it is not preceded by ignorance, does not change when things change (objects are known before existence), and temporal distinctions do not affect it. Any scriptural expressions that might imply otherwise (like Surah 2:143) are interpreted metaphorically (ta'wīl). Second, a subtle point: the term 'inkishāf' (disclosure) might suggest temporality since it is a passive verb form, but in definitions, verbs are timeless. Still, some consider the term inappropriate because it implies an act of unveiling after concealment. Third, Allah's knowledge is self-reflexive: He knows His knowledge through His knowledge, as well as His essence and other attributes, because attributes not of effecting can relate to themselves. Fourth, the definition's phrase 'as they are' indicates the true nature of known things. Fifth, 'disclosure that does not admit its opposite' excludes conjecture, belief, and illusion, which are uncertain.

Key Terms
ʿilm
azaliyya
wājib
jāʾiz
mustaḥīl

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes as events occur and as time passes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that time is a property of known things, not knowledge; divine knowledge is eternal and unchanging, encompassing past, present, and future in one timeless act.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'encompassing all things' in the definition of Allah's knowledge?
A) Only existing objects and events.
B) Only necessary beings.
C) Necessary, possible, and impossible things.
D) Only what will exist in the future.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is eternal, all-encompassing, and unchanging, and any scriptural expressions that suggest otherwise require metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you remember that Allah knew that sin before you even committed it, and yet He gives you chance after chance? How does that knowledge transform your relationship with your own failures?
?
If Allah's knowledge of your future is already perfect and unchanging, why do you still cling to your own plans as if they determine your fate? What would it take for you to trust His knowledge more than your own plotting?
?
You worry about things that may never happen—yet Allah already knows every outcome with absolute certainty. Can you list one worry you have right now and consciously hand it back to Allah, acknowledging that He knows it better than you? What stops you?

Refining the Definition of Knowledge
Refinements and clarifications on the definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic refinements in the definition of Allah's knowledge, including the problem of 'inkishāf', the self-reflexivity of divine knowledge, and the exclusion of conjecture.

Core Idea
Every word in defining Allah's attributes is chosen to avoid unworthy implications.

After establishing the basic definition of Allah's knowledge, the text addresses subtle but important points. It examines the word 'inkishāf' (disclosure) and its potential temporal implication, explains that Allah knows His own knowledge, and clarifies that the definition excludes uncertain mental states like conjecture and illusion.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the problem of 'inkishāf'
Read the objection: the word 'inkishāf' suggests a new manifestation after concealment. Note the answer: verbs in definitions are timeless, though some consider the term problematic anyway.
This shows the extreme care taken to avoid even the slightest hint of temporality in Allah.
2
Understand self-reflexive knowledge
The passage states that Allah knows His knowledge through His knowledge. Reflect on how this is possible only for attributes that are not attributes of effecting (ta'thīr).
3
Identify what is excluded by the definition
The final note explains that the phrase 'does not admit of its opposite' excludes conjecture (ẓann), belief (iʿtiqād), and illusion (wahm) which are uncertain.
This distinguishes divine knowledge from human opinion.

?

?

Classification of Divine Attributes: Negative, Essential, and Meaningful
Classification of divine attributes into negative, essential, and meaningful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians classify divine attributes into negative (salbiyya), essential (nafsiyya), and meaningful (ma'nawiyya) categories based on their logical relationship to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are classified by whether they negate, are essential, or depend on a cause.

In this passage, Imam Dardir presents a logical classification of divine attributes. He distinguishes between attributes that negate what is unfitting for God (salbiyya), attributes that are necessary for the essence itself (nafsiyya), and attributes that depend on a cause (ma'nawiyya). Understanding this classification is essential for proper theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the flow from non-existential attributes to the three categories.
2
Identify the three categories
Find where the passage defines negative, essential, and meaningful attributes. Note the conditions each category has.
The passage uses 'إن لم تكن وجوديّة' as the starting point.
3
Compare nafsiyya and ma'nawiyya
What distinguishes an essential attribute from a meaningful one? Look at the condition about the cause.
The key difference is whether the attribute is necessary as long as the essence exists or only as long as its cause exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم تكن وجوديّةً، فإنْ كان مدلولها عدَمَ أمر لا يليقُ سُمّيت سلبية، وإن معللة بعلة لم يكُنْ مدلولها عدَما، فإن كانتْ واجبةً للذاتِ - ما دامتِ الذاتُ غَيرَ - سُمّيت صفةً نفسيّةً وحالا نفْسيّةً، كالوُجودِ وكالتحيز للجرْمِ وقبوله للأغراض . وإن كانت معللةً بعلة بأن كانتْ واجبةً للذات ما دامت علتُها سُمّيت معنوية كالعالمية والقادرية، أي: كون الذات الموصوفةِ بالعِلْمِ عالمة، وكَونِ الذات الموصوفة بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي (سبعة للرائي) أي: الناظر المتأمل.
And if it (an attribute) is not existential, then if its signification is the negation of an unfitting thing, it is called negative. And if it is caused by a cause (mu'allala bi-illa) and its signification is not a negation, then if it is necessary for the essence as long as the essence does not change, it is called an essential attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) and a state (ḥāl nafsiyya), like existence (wujūd) and like occupying space (taḥayyuz) for a body (jirm) and its receptivity to accidents. And if it is caused by a cause, i.e., it is necessary for the essence as long as its cause exists, it is called a meaningful attribute (ṣifa ma'nawiyya), like being omniscient (al-ʿālimiyya) and being omnipotent (al-qādiriyya), i.e., the essence described by knowledge is knowing, and the essence described by power is powerful, in relation to the meanings (maʿānī), and they are (seven for the observer?) meaning the one who looks and reflects.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes based on their logical relationship to the essence. It begins by considering two main categories: existential (wujūdiyya) and non-existential. For non-existential attributes, if they signify a negation of something unfitting, they are called negative (salbiyya). If the attribute is caused (mu'allala bi-illa) but does not signify a negation, a further division is made: if the attribute is necessary for the essence as long as the essence remains unchanged, it is called an essential attribute (nafsiyya) and described as a state (ḥāl). Examples include existence itself and the property of occupying space for a body. If the attribute is necessary for the essence only as long as its cause exists, it is called a meaningful attribute (ma'nawiyya), such as being omniscient or omnipotent. This means that the essence is described by these attributes in relation to the underlying meanings (maʿānī). The passage ends with a reference to the meanings being seven for the one who reflects, though the exact phrase is unclear.

Key Terms
salbiyya
nafsiyya
ḥāl
ma'nawiyya
ʿālimiyya
qādiriyya
maʿānī

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that essential attributes (nafsiyya) are identical to the essence itself.
Correction: Rather, they are necessary concomitants of the essence, not identical. The passage says they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a meaningful (ma'nawiyya) attribute from an essential (nafsiyya) attribute?
A) A ma'nawiyya attribute is not caused by a cause.
B) A ma'nawiyya attribute depends on a cause that can change, while a nafsiyya attribute is necessary as long as the essence exists.
C) A ma'nawiyya attribute is negative, while nafsiyya is existential.
D) A ma'nawiyya attribute is only for created beings.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are logically classified into negative (negating unfitting), essential (necessary for essence), and meaningful (dependent on a cause).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that meaningful attributes (like al-ʿālimiyya) are caused by a cause (knowledge), do you feel that God's omniscience is now reduced to a secondary quality? How does this classification challenge or strengthen your reliance on God's attributes in your daily supplications?
?
The lesson explains that essential attributes (nafsiyya) are necessary for the essence as long as the essence does not change. If God's essence is unchanging, how does this affect your confidence in God's attributes being constant? Is there any attribute of God that you have doubted in a moment of hardship? How does this classification help you reaffirm it?
?
Consider that negative attributes (salbiyya) merely negate imperfections. Have you ever attributed to God a quality that is actually a negation of something unfitting? How does reframing it refine your worship and deepen your understanding of God's transcendence?

The Divine Attribute of Life
Definition of Allah's life as an eternal attribute prerequisite for knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of life as an eternal prerequisite for knowledge and will, and how it differs from human life.

Core Idea
Allah's life is the eternal foundation upon which all other attributes rest.

This passage defines the attribute of life (ḥayāt) as an eternal quality of Allah that is the condition for knowledge, will, and power. Unlike our life, it is not based on a soul or body. We'll explore this definition and the logical chain of dependency among the divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the definition.
The passage begins with the matn line mentioning life, power, and will.
2
Identify the core definition
Find how the passage defines life: 'an eternal attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and will.' Notice that life is not defined as something we can fully comprehend.
The footnote clarifies it is not like human life based on a soul.
3
Understand the dependency chain
The second footnote explains that power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge. Thus life is the condition (sharṭ ṣiḥḥa) for all these attributes.
This shows life is foundational for other essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-۳۳ حياته، وقُدْرةٌ ، إرادة * وكُلُّ شَيء كائن أرادَة - و (حياته) تعالى، وهي: صفةٌ أزليّةٌ تُوجبُ صِحةَ العِلم والإرادة . (۱) الحياة صفة أزلية لا يعلم كنهها أحد إلا الله وليست بسبب روح كحياتنا. (۲) والمراد أن العلم والقدرة والإرادة متوقفة على الحياة، لأن تعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم فهي شرط صحة فيما ذكره وغيره من الصفات. [حاشية السباعي، ص ١٠٠]
-33 His life, power, will * and everything that occurs [is by] His will - And (His life) exalted be He, which is an eternal attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and will. (1) Life is an eternal attribute whose reality none knows except Allah, and it is not due to a soul like our life. (2) What is meant is that knowledge, power, and will depend on life, because the attachment of power follows the attachment of will, and the attachment of will follows the attachment of knowledge, so it is a condition of soundness for these and other attributes. [Hashiyat al-Sabbagh, p. 100]
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life (al-ḥayāt). It begins by citing the matn line mentioning life, power, and will. The definition states: 'an eternal attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and will.' In other words, life is the prerequisite condition for knowledge and will to be valid attributes of God. The first footnote clarifies that life is not like human life based on a soul; rather, it is an eternal attribute whose true essence is known only to Allah. The second footnote explains the logical dependency: power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge, so life is the foundation for all these attributes. This establishes life as a necessary condition (sharṭ ṣiḥḥa) for the other essential attributes of action and volition.

Key Terms
ḥayāt
azaliyya
sharṭ ṣiḥḥa
ʿilm
irāda
qudra

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Allah's life is like human life, based on a soul or body.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that life is an eternal attribute whose reality none knows except Allah, and it is not due to a soul like our life.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between life, knowledge, and will according to this passage?
A) Life is an effect of knowledge and will.
B) Life is a condition for the soundness of knowledge and will.
C) Knowledge and will are prerequisites for life.
D) Life is the same as power.

Key Takeaway
Life is an eternal attribute that is a necessary condition for the soundness of knowledge, will, and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's life is the foundation of your own ability to know, will, and act, does your daily reliance on these faculties make you turn to Him with gratitude and dependence? Where do you feel you've assumed your own independence?
?
The passage says the reality of Allah's life is known only to Him. Does that humble your mind when you try to picture or define Him? Will you let that mystery deepen your awe rather than reduce Him to your imagination?
?
Your own life is a temporary gift from the One whose life is eternal. How should that reshape your attachment to this world? What would it look like to truly live today as someone whose life is borrowed from the Ever-Living?

The Definition of Divine Power (Qudra)
Definition of Allah's power as the ability to bring about or remove possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of Allah's power as an eternal attribute enabling the creation and annihilation of possible things.

Core Idea
Allah's power is the ability to bring about or remove any possible thing.

This lesson begins with the core definition of divine power from Imam Dardir's text. It establishes that power is an eternal attribute, not a temporal ability, and its specific relation to possible (mumkin) things. Understanding this definition is foundational for avoiding misconceptions about God's omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic sentence defining power. Notice the words 'eternal attribute' and 'possible' – these are key.
The definition is concise; each word carries technical weight.
2
Understand 'possible' (mumkin)
Power is related only to possible things. Ask: what is a possible thing? (Something whose existence and non-existence are both logically permissible).
Necessary and impossible are excluded, as we will see later.
3
Contrast with human power
Human power is temporal and limited; divine power is eternal and unrestricted. Reflect on why the text insists on 'eternal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وقُدْرة) وهي: صفةٌ أزليّةً يتأتى بها إيجاد الممكن وإعدامه. (۳) المراد بالقدرة التمكن من الفعل والترك، لأنه تعالى فاعل مختار، والفاعل المختار هو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك. (٤) فوائد ١ دخل في هذا التعريف أفعالنا الاختيارية، ففيه رد على المعتزلة القائلين بأن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية، ودخل أيضا ما له سبب كالإحراق عند مماسة النار الشيء المحرق، وما لا سبب له كخلق السماوات والأرض. -٢- نسبة التأثير للقدرة مجاز، وإلا فالمؤثر إنما هو الذات. خرج بالممكن الواجب فإنها لو تعلقت به من حيث الوجود لزم تحصيل الحاصل، أو من حيث عدمه فهو عليه محال، وخرج أيضا المستحيل فلا تتعلق به القدرة، وهذا ليس عجزا لأن القدرة لا تتعلق به، ولا يلزم العجز إلا لو كان عدم التعلق معنى يرجع للقدرة. - تتعلق القدرة بالإيجاد والإعدام، هذا هو المشهور، وقيل: لا تتعلق بالإعدام بل إذا أراد الله تعالى إعدام شيء أمسك عنه المدد. - اعلم أن أعدامنا الأزلية لا تتعلق بها القدرة ولا الإرادة اتفاقا لوجوبها، وأما أعدامنا فيما لا يزال السابقة على وجودنا، ووجودنا بعد عدمنا، واستمرار وجودنا، وإعدامنا بعد وجودنا، وإيجادنا يوم القيامة فمن تعلقات القدرة والإرادة -٦- التعاريف في صفات الباري جل وعلا ليست حدودًا حقيقة، وإنما هي رسوم لأنه لا يعلم كنه ذاته وصفاته إلا هو. انتهى ملخصا ومرتبا . [ حاشية الصاوي ص ٤٥ ، حاشية السباعي ص ١٠٠]
(And power) which is an eternal attribute by which the bringing into existence (ījād) and annihilation (iʿdām) of the possible (al-mumkin) are accomplished. (3) What is meant by power is the ability (tamakkun) to act and to refrain, because He is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), and the free agent is one who if He wills does, and if He wills refrains. (4) Benefits: 1) This definition includes our voluntary actions, and it is a refutation of the Muʿtazila who say that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. It also includes what has a cause, like burning upon contact with fire on a burnable thing, and what has no cause, like the creation of the heavens and earth. 2) The attribution of effect (ta'thīr) to power is metaphorical; otherwise, the true agent is the essence. The necessary (wājib) is excluded from the object of power, for if it were related to it in terms of existence it would entail obtaining the already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), or in terms of non-existence it is impossible. Also, the impossible (mustaḥīl) is excluded, for power is not related to it, and this is not impotence because power is not of a nature to be related to it; impotence would only follow if the lack of relation were due to a deficiency in the power. Power is related to bringing into existence and annihilation; this is the well-known view. It is said that it is not related to annihilation; rather, when Allah wills to annihilate something, He withholds preservation (imdād). Know that our eternal non-existences (i.e., prior to creation) are not related to power or will by consensus because they are necessary (as non-existent). However, our non-existences in the future that precede our existence, our existence after non-existence, the continuation of our existence, our annihilation after existence, and our re-creation on the Day of Judgment are among the objects of power and will. 6) Definitions of the attributes of the Creator are not true definitions (ḥudūd haqīqiyya) but only descriptions (rusūm), because no one knows the true reality of His essence and attributes except Him. Finished, summarized and arranged. [Hashiyat al-Ṣāwī, p. 45; Hashiyat al-Sabbagh, p. 100]
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of power (al-qudra). It states: 'an eternal attribute by which the bringing into existence and annihilation of the possible are accomplished.' The first footnote explains that power is the ability to act or refrain, which is why God is described as a free agent. Then follows a list of benefits (fawāʾid). The first benefit: the definition includes human voluntary actions, thus refuting the Muʿtazilite view that humans create their own actions; it also covers caused and uncaused events. The second benefit: attributing effect (ta'thīr) to power is metaphorical; the true agent is the divine essence. The objects of power are limited to possible things: necessary and impossible things are excluded because relation to them would entail absurdities. The lack of power over the impossible is not impotence. The passage then discusses whether power relates to annihilation: the common view is yes, but some say God simply withholds preservation. It also clarifies which non-existences are subject to power (future non-existences before existence, etc.) versus those that are not (eternal non-existences). Finally, it notes that definitions of divine attributes are only descriptive (rusūm), not true definitions, because God's essence and attributes are unknowable in their true reality.

Key Terms
qudra
mumkin
ījād
iʿdām

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is like human power, temporal and limited.
Correction: The attribute of power is eternal and not dependent on time or capacity.

Checkpoint
What does the definition of power (qudra) specifically relate to?
A) All things, including necessary and impossible.
B) Only possible things (mumkin).
C) Only human actions.
D) Only the creation of the heavens and earth.

Key Takeaway
Allah's power is an eternal attribute exclusively directed towards possible things, not necessary or impossible ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation you desperately want to change, do you truly believe that Allah's power extends over every possible outcome—including the one you fear most? What would it look like to live today with that certainty?
?
Allah's power includes both giving and taking away. Is there something in your life you cling to as if it were guaranteed? How might you realign your heart to accept that His power to remove is as perfect as His power to give?
?
If Allah is the free agent who does as He wills and refrains as He wills, where in your life have you assumed He must act according to your expectations? Will you surrender that expectation to His will right now?

Power and Human Actions: Refuting the Muʿtazila
Benefit: the definition includes human actions, refuting Mu'tazila, and covers caused/uncaused events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of divine power includes human voluntary actions, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
All human actions are under God's power, not created by the servant.

The previous lesson defined power in general. Now a key benefit is drawn: this definition covers human voluntary actions, thereby refuting the Mu'tazila who held that humans create their own deeds. The text also extends power to both caused and uncaused events, affirming God's absolute control.

Guided Path
1
Read the benefit
Read the Arabic sentence starting with 'دخل في هذا التعريف أفعالنا الاختيارية'. Understand that it claims the definition includes our voluntary actions.
The term 'voluntary actions' means actions done with choice.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila believed humans create their own actions, which would limit God's power. The text says the correct definition includes them under God's power, refuting that view.
This is a major theological debate in Islam.
3
Note the inclusiveness
The definition also includes both caused events (like fire burning) and uncaused events (like the creation of heavens). Reflect on why this matters.

The Scope and Limits of Divine Power
Exclusion of necessary and impossible, metaphorical attribution, and definitions as descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessary and impossible things are excluded from divine power, and why this exclusion does not imply weakness.

Core Idea
Power only relates to possibles; not relating to impossibles is not impotence.

Having established that power relates to possibles, the text now explains what is excluded: necessary things (like God Himself) and impossible things (like a partner with God). The exclusion of impossibles is not a sign of weakness, because the impossible is not an object of power by nature. The passage also clarifies that the attribution of effect to power is metaphorical, and notes that definitions of God are only descriptive.

Guided Path
1
Read the exclusion of necessary and impossible
Read the Arabic from 'خرج بالممكن الواجب' to 'فهو عليه محال'. Understand that power cannot relate to necessary or impossible things.
Necessary cannot be brought into existence (already exists) and impossible cannot exist at all.
2
Understand why not relating to impossibles is not weakness
The text says 'ليس عجزا' because the lack of relation is due to the nature of the object, not a deficiency in power.
Impotence would be if power were supposed to affect it but failed.
3
Learn about metaphorical attribution of effect
Read 'نسبة التأثير للقدرة مجاز'. The true agent is the essence; power is just the attribute through which effect occurs metaphorically.
This prevents ascribing independent effect to attributes.
4
Reflect on the final note about definitions
Read the concluding paragraph about rusūm. Understand that we cannot truly define God's essence; we only describe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot do the impossible, so He is not all-powerful.
Correction: The impossible is not a possible object; not relating to it does not limit power. Omnipotence means power over all possibles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not a sign of impotence that power does not relate to the impossible?
A) Because God chooses not to relate to it.
B) Because the impossible is not an object that power could be related to by nature.
C) Because God's power is limited to what He wills.
D) Because the impossible does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Divine power excludes necessary and impossible things by definition, and this exclusion is not a limitation but a logical necessity; true definitions of Allah are beyond human grasp.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever internally questioned God's power when facing an 'impossible' situation? How does this lesson confront your expectation that God should be able to do anything—even logical absurdities—and what change does it call for in your trust in Him?
?
If definitions of Allah are only descriptions (rusūm), are you content to leave the deep mysteries of His essence unknown, or are you secretly trying to reduce Him to something you can fully understand? What would it mean for your worship to truly accept that He is beyond definition?
?
When you attribute an achievement or failure to yourself, have you paused to remember that the true agent of all effect is Allah alone? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that your 'power' is purely metaphorical, and how would that reshape your humility and reliance on Him?

Definition of the Divine Attribute of Life
Dardeer defines the attribute of Life and explains its role as prerequisite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attribute of Life is an eternal prerequisite for knowledge, power, and will, and is unlike human life.

Core Idea
Divine Life is the eternal root of all divine knowledge and will.

This lesson introduces the divine attribute of Life (al-Ḥayāh) as defined by Imam Dardīr. The passage clarifies that this life is not a physical soul but an eternal attribute that makes other attributes possible. Understanding this helps avoid anthropomorphism and establishes the foundation for Allah's active attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two numbered marginal notes.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Define the attribute of Life
From the first line, Life is an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and will. Write down the definition.
The term 'necessitates' (tūjibu) is key.
3
Distinguish divine life from human life
The first marginal note clarifies that divine life is not due to a soul. Contrast with human life which is caused.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
4
Map the dependency of attributes
The second marginal note shows that power depends on will, will depends on knowledge, and all depend on life. Create a diagram or list.
Life is the foundation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (حياته)) تعالى، وهي: صفةٌ أزليّةٌ تُوجبُ صِحةَ العِلم والإرادة. (۱) الحياة صفة أزلية لا يعلم كنهها أحد إلا الله وليست بسبب روح كحياتنا. (۲) والمراد أن العلم والقدرة والإرادة متوقفة على الحياة، لأن تعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم فهي شرط صحة فيما ذكره وغيره من الصفات.
And His Life, Exalted is He: it is an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and will. (1) Life is an eternal attribute whose essence no one knows except Allah, and it is not due to a soul like our life. (2) The intended meaning is that knowledge, power, and will depend on life, because the attachment of power follows the attachment of will, and the attachment of will follows the attachment of knowledge; thus it is a condition of validity for the mentioned attributes and others.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of Life (Hayah). The author begins by stating that Life is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) that makes knowledge and will valid—meaning that without life, knowledge and will cannot exist in the divine essence. The marginal notes clarify that this life is not like human life which is caused by a soul; rather it is an attribute whose true nature is known only to Allah. Furthermore, life is a prerequisite for knowledge, power, and will: the attachment of power depends on the attachment of will, and will depends on knowledge. This hierarchical dependence shows that life is foundational for the other attributes of action and cognition.

Key Terms

صفة أزلية (ṣifa azaliyya)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الإرادة (al-irāda)
القدرة (al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine life is like human life, caused by a soul.
Correction: Divine life is eternal and not caused; its true nature is known only to Allah.

A) Life is a consequence of Knowledge
B) Life is independent of the other attributes
C) Life is a prerequisite for Knowledge, Power, and Will
D) Life is identical to Power

Key Takeaway
Life is the foundational eternal attribute without which Allah's knowledge, will, and power cannot be valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If Allah's life is absolutely foundational, then every moment you exist is a direct reflection of His giving you life from His life. Does your daily gratitude acknowledge this gift as not just a given, but as a constant, intentional act of the Creator? Commit now to a specific way you will thank Him tomorrow morning for your own life.
